Chapter 1: 山田某氏

The fluorescent lights of the 7-Eleven on the corner of Shinjuku-dori flickered in that way they always did just before midnight—three rapid stutters, a pause, then steady. Yamada Boushi knew that pattern as intimately as she knew the price of a cup of instant ramen (¥108 on sale, ¥128 when she was feeling decadent) and the exact number of steps from her apartment to the station (1,247, if she took the shortcut through the love hotel alley).

She was twenty-six years old, unmarried, and earning exactly ¥2,500,000 per year at a small trading company where her official title was “general affairs assistant” and her unofficial job description was “the person who makes the tea and handles the spreadsheets neither of the section chiefs understand.” The tea came from a bag. The spreadsheets came from her soul.

Tonight, like most nights, she had worked until the last train, because the senior section chief had “urgently needed” a reconciliation of the department’s stationery budget from fiscal 2018, and no one else had been willing to stay. She had done it in forty-three minutes. She had also, during her lunch break, executed a multi-currency rebalancing of ¥280 billion worth of emerging-market small-cap stocks, approved three new client onboards from a human trafficker’s referral network in Ho Chi Minh City, and filed a suspicious activity report so carefully worded that the Financial Intelligence Division would file it under “genuinely bewildering” and forget about it.

Her life, in other words, was an elaborate magic trick. One part visible. One part invisible. And the invisible part held ¥2.8 trillion.

The train was mercifully empty. She found a seat by the window, rested her forehead against the cool glass, and let her eyes close for just a moment. The train rattled through a tunnel. Her phone, which served as her terminal, buzzed softly in her pocket—a system alert from the AI. She ignored it. It could wait until she got home.

Home was a 1K apartment in Suginami-ku, six tatami mats in the main room, a kitchen the size of a coat closet, and a monthly rent of ¥68,000. The walls were thin enough to hear the neighbor’s NHK subscription fee argument through them. The bath had a timer that shut off after fifteen minutes. The view was of the neighbouring building’s air conditioner units.

But the closet—the tiny, dusty closet by the genkan—that was special.

She slid the door open, pushed aside a stack of old yukata she’d never worn, and pressed her thumb to the biometric reader hidden in the wall. A seam appeared. A panel clicked open. Behind it, humming softly in the dark, sat the beating heart of 株式会社某社.

Three servers, custom-built, liquid-cooled, running a cluster of neural nets that had been trained on forty years of global market data and coded in a proprietary language that made standard financial software weep with inadequacy. The AI—she called it “小町” after the Heian poet, because both were elegant and terrifying—ran the portfolio. Boushi ran the rest.

There was no office. No staff. No corporate headquarters in a Roppongi high-rise with a view of Tokyo Tower. There was just this closet, a dedicated fiber line that she had installed herself (breaking three fire codes and her spirit in the process), and a five-year-old laptop that she had bought refurbished from a shop in Akihabara.

The plaque hung above the servers, in a cheap frame from Daiso.


「年金は人権なり」

For a long moment, she stood and read it, as she did every night, as if she were checking that the words hadn’t faded. They never did. The ink was permanent marker, her own handwriting, and she had traced it so many times that the paper had begun to yellow where her thumb had pressed.

Behind her eyes, unbidden, came the memory.

It had been a Tuesday in February. The kind of cold that settled into the bones and refused to leave, even though the gas heater in the living room wheezed at maximum output. She was fifteen. Her mother had been coughing for three weeks, a wet, rattling sound that Boushi could still hear in the silence of her apartment on quiet nights.

The doctor at the small clinic in their neighborhood—Dr. Tanaka, a tired man with nicotine-stained fingers—had taken Boushi aside in the hallway while her mother sat in the examination room, pretending not to listen.

“It’s pneumonia,” he had said, not unkindly. “Bacterial. With proper antibiotics, she’ll recover in a week. But—”

The but had hung in the air like a bird taking flight. Boushi had known. She had known, even then, at fifteen, from the way her mother’s nails had been unpainted for six months, from the way the rice cooker had been turned off for dinner three nights in a row, from the way her mother had stopped buying the weekly NHK broadcast fee ticket and started pretending no one was home when the collector came.

“We can schedule treatment at the university hospital,” Dr. Tanaka had continued, writing something on a prescription pad that he didn’t hand over. “But you’ll need to pay the deposit first. The national insurance covers the rest, but the hospital requires an upfront admission fee of ¥150,000.”

¥150,000.

They had ¥23,000 in the emergency envelope in the kitchen drawer. Her mother had been working part-time at a combini, earning ¥70,000 a month after deductions, and the landlord had already given them a final notice for two months’ back rent.

Boushi had nodded. She had thanked the doctor. She had taken her mother home, made her soup from a packet, and put her to bed. And then she had called every relative she knew, every friend of the family, and listened to the same sequence of apologies and excuses until she had run out of people to call.

Her mother had died fourteen days later, in the rented hospital bed that they had managed to get through a charity program—but only after the pneumonia had spread to both lungs, and the antibiotics, when they finally found them, had been too little, too late.

The last thing her mother had said to her, voice barely a whisper, throat raw from coughing: “Gomen ne. ごめんね、某氏。こんな親で。Such a useless mother.”

Boushi had held her hand until it was cold. She had stayed in the room until the nurse came to draw the curtain. She had walked home in the rain without an umbrella, and she had not cried.

She had not cried the next day, either, when she had to call her school to explain she would be absent for an indefinite period. She had not cried when the social worker came and asked about guardianship. She had not cried when she filled out the forms for the bereaved family pension, which came to ¥58,000 a month, barely enough to cover rent.

She had started to plan.

Someone had to make sure that no one else died because they couldn’t afford ¥150,000 for an antibiotic. Someone had to build a system that treated access to a living income—a pension, a safety net, the dignity of knowing that you would not be left to die in a rented bed that you couldn’t pay for—as what it was.

Not a luxury. Not a government priority. Not a line item in a budget.

A human right.

The alert on her phone buzzed again, and Boushi blinked, the memory dissolving like steam on a mirror. She pulled out the phone—a three-year-old model with a cracked screen that she had refused to replace because it was ¥15,000 she could better spend on server cooling—and checked the notification.

`KOMACHI_ALERT: TARGET REBALANCE EXECUTED. SECTOR DRIFT CORRECTED. NEW POSITION: ANGLO-ASIAN MINERALS LTD (AIM: AAML). $2.3M. ADDITIONAL NOTE: COMPANY CEO REPORTEDLY ACCUSED OF FORCED LABOR IN MYANMAR. RECOMMEND DIVESTMENT BY Q2 UNLESS REMEDIATION CONFIRMED.`

Boushi typed a quick reply: *Hold. Monitor. Initiate engagement protocol if no remediation by March 31.* The AI would parse the instruction, log the decision, and update the proxy voting policy automatically. She had long ago learned that the best way to change corporate behavior was not to sell shares but to use them. Voting rights. Shareholder proposals. A polite but relentless pressure that looked like a whisper but felt like a drill.

That was the trick of the whole operation. Invisible pressure. Quiet leverage. A fund that held over fifteen thousand stocks—way too many for any traditional fund to track, but child’s play for a neural net—and charged an expense ratio of 0.025% per year. One basis point to Boushi’s salary and expenses. One basis point to the yakuza and triads who provided the infrastructure for the clients who couldn’t use banks. Half a basis point to trading fees and AI upgrades.

The clients came from the shadows. Former Cold War informants who had been promised citizenship and received a pat on the back. Drug dealers who wanted to retire without getting robbed. Hostesses on tourist visas who couldn’t open a bank account. Hitmen whose employers had stopped answering their calls. All of them, everyone the legitimate system had discarded or exploited, anyone who had money they couldn’t safely hold—the fund took it. Invested it. Let it grow. And paid it out only when they retired, or when they had a medical emergency.

No withdrawals for lifestyle. No early cash-outs. You wanted the money? You proved you needed it for surgery, for hospice, for the one thing that money could actually buy: the chance to live long enough to become someone’s problem.

The fund had never lost a client to treatable illness.

She had made sure of it.

There was a separate medical fund—a ¥12 billion sub-portfolio—that paid out same-day, no questions asked, on submission of a doctor’s note and a photograph of the hospital invoice. Boushi personally reviewed every claim. She had been known to approve payments from her own phone while standing in line at the combini. She had been known to call the hospital to negotiate discounts on behalf of patients she had never met, in languages she had learned from YouTube videos and subtitled dramas.

She had been known, once, to fly to Thailand for a weekend—on a budget airline, seat 37F, no checked luggage—to deliver a medical visa application in person, because the client’s Thai passport had expired and the Japanese consulate had been unsympathetic. She had handed the envelope to the client’s daughter outside a hotel in Bangkok, bowed, and caught the red-eye back.

That had cost ¥89,000. She had accounted for it under “business development.”

The train pulled into her station. She got off, walked the 1,247 steps home, and let herself into the apartment. The closet hummed a familiar song. She changed out of her work clothes—a ¥6,000 blazer from Uniqlo, a ¥2,000 skirt from a discount chain, sensible pumps that had been resoled twice—and into a faded tracksuit that had once been blue and was now a kind of wash of memory.

She poured a glass of tap water and opened the laptop, sitting cross-legged on the tatami. The fund’s dashboard showed a green number: ¥2,812,134,567,891. A tiny uptick from yesterday. Good. The AI had been active, shifting allocations, capturing small inefficiencies. The average holding was ¥188 million, which meant most positions were tiny—a fraction of a percentage point. But when you owned fifteen thousand stocks, you didn’t need any single one to be a home run. You just needed the market to keep growing, and for your costs to be so low that the growth went to the clients, not to the manager.

The average Japanese pension fund charged 1.5% in expenses. The average mutual fund charged 2–3%. The world’s largest index fund, the benchmark that Boushi’s fund was designed to beat, charged 0.03% for a simple market-cap-weighted portfolio of a few thousand stocks.

She beat it anyway. By a consistent forty to sixty basis points a year, because she didn’t pay index licensing fees, because she held more stocks than any index, because she didn’t use leverage or derivatives, and because she had no compliance department, no HR department, no rent, no coffee budget, and no quarterly off-site retreats in Hakone.

She had a ¥68,000 apartment, a ¥15,000 clothing budget per year, and a pension fund that was, by any ethical and legal standard, possibly the most effective investment vehicle for the dispossessed ever created.

“仕方がない,” she said to the empty room. “Someone has to do it.”

The plaque caught the light from the laptop screen.

「年金は人権なり」

She had written those words the night she had finished coding the AI, three years ago, in a rented internet café in Akihabara because her apartment had no electricity. She had been twenty-three, alone, and terrified that she had just wasted ¥400,000 of the initial seed money on servers that might explode.

The seed money had come from an unexpected source: a Chinese former spy who had defected in the 1980s, been granted asylum in Japan, and then been abandoned by the government with no pension and no job. He had been working as a dishwasher when Boushi met him—she had been volunteering at a soup kitchen on weekends—and he had told her his story over a bowl of miso soup.

“In the end,” he had said, his Japanese rough but clear, “no one remembers what you did for them. They only remember if you were there when they needed you.”

He had given her ¥5 million in cash, stuffed in a paper bag. “Do something with it. Don’t waste it on food for me. Make it so no one ends up like me.”

She had invested that ¥5 million according to the strategy she had been developing since she was fifteen. She had run it through a brute-force backtester she had coded herself, using a desktop computer in the school library after closing hours. She had turned ¥5 million into ¥7 million in six months. Then ¥10 million. Then ¥50 million.

Word had spread, in the silent channels that exist between people who have no legal identity. A yakuza underboss in Kabukicho had approached her, not with threats, but with a proposition: eyes and ears on the ground, protection from extortion, and a place to keep the cash that the legitimate banks would not touch. In return, they would take a point of the AUM per year.

She had said yes. She had had no choice. The fund needed volume to work—it was a numbers game, a scale game—and she needed clients who could not open accounts anywhere else. The yakuza provided the network. She provided the returns.

Another point went to them. She kept one for herself. The numbers worked.

Now, three years later, she answered to no one and to many. The Diet was aware of the fund’s existence—she had made sure of that, through a carefully placed letter to a sympathetic junior lawmaker who sat on the Social Security Committee—but had chosen not to act. The fund was too useful, too quiet, too unthreatening. It served people the government had already failed. Let it exist, the unwritten policy said. Just don’t make it loud.

She had kept it quiet. She had kept it invisible. And she had kept it alive, even as the hedge funds and the banks and the regulators began to sniff around the edges of the market, wondering where all this liquidity was coming from, who was buying the tiny small caps at such consistent volume, and why the pattern didn’t match any known institutional strategy.

Sooner or later, they would find her. She knew that. The fund was too large to stay hidden forever—¥2.8 trillion had a gravity that eventually pulled in attention. But she had prepared for that too.

A dead man’s switch, coded into the AI’s deepest layer. If her biometrics went silent for more than seventy-two hours, if she missed two consecutive check-ins, if anyone tried to access the servers without her physical thumbprint and a matching retinal scan, the entire client list—names, former affiliations, current investments, geographic locations—would be sent to the news desks of every major English- and Japanese-language newspaper in the world.

Let them come, she thought. Let them try to shut it down. They would have to explain to the public why a fund that had never committed a crime—that had, in fact, paid its taxes on time and in full—should be frozen, when the alternative was fifteen thousand people suddenly discovering they had no savings, no medical fund, no future.

The dead man’s switch was not a threat. It was a guarantee.

She closed the laptop at 1:47 AM. Tomorrow, she would go to work, make the tea, reconcile the stationery budget, and smile at everyone who called her “山田さん” without knowing that her given name was literally “Anonymous” and that she held more assets under her personal management than some sovereign wealth funds.

Tomorrow, she would do it all again.

But tonight, she lay on her futon, turned off the light, and listened to the servers hum in the closet. The sound was like a heartbeat. The sound of something that should not exist, working anyway.

Outside, the city glowed orange. Inside, she closed her eyes, and for a few blessed hours, she did not dream of hospital beds.

She dreamed of the plaque.

「年金は人権なり」

She had meant it.

She still meant it.

## Continuity Notes

- **Character introduction:** Yamada Boushi, age 26, OL at a small trading company, secret manager of 株式会社某社 pension fund (¥2.8T AUM).
- **Key backstory established:** Mother died of bacterial pneumonia at age 15 due to inability to pay ¥150,000 hospital deposit. This is the foundation of Boushi’s motivation.
- **Fund operations established:** AI named “小町” (Komachi), low-cost MER breakdown (0.025%, split between salary/yakuza/trading&Ai), dead man’s switch, medical fund (¥12B sub-portfolio), client base (dispossessed/criminal).
- **Plaque introduced:** 「年金は人権なり」 hangs above servers.
- **Fund origin:** Seed money from former Chinese spy (defector, 1980s) met at soup kitchen; initial ¥5M grown via backtester.
- **Relationship with yakuza:** Protective arrangement, they receive 1 bp of AUM annually.
- **Government awareness:** Diet aware but passive; junior lawmaker on Social Security Committee has been contacted.
- **Tone set:** Satirical (the closet, the OL cover), heart (mother’s death, mission), character-driven (Boushi’s voice, her frugality and dedication).
- **No subplots active yet** (first chapter).

Chapter 2: The Envelope

The envelope arrived on a Tuesday.

It was slipped under the door of Boushi's apartment sometime between the hours of three and five in the morning, when the city's rhythms slowed to a crawl and the only sounds were the distant wail of a police siren and the hum of the servers in the closet. She found it when she woke at six, as she always did, padding barefoot to the genkan to collect the morning newspaper that she never read.

The envelope was cream-colored, heavy-weight stock, the kind that cost more per sheet than Boushi spent on a week's worth of lunches. No stamp. No address. Just her name, written in black ink with a fountain pen, the strokes deliberate and precise:

*山田某氏様*

She picked it up as if it might bite her. The paper had a weight that suggested more than a single sheet inside. She turned it over. The seal was a blob of crimson wax, stamped with a character she recognized immediately: **龍**.

Dragon.

There were only three yakuza families in Tokyo that used that seal. Boushi knew all of them by reputation and by spreadsheet. The fund held deposits from twelve of their subsidiaries, representing roughly ¥240 billion in AUM. She had met exactly one of their senior members—a man in his sixties named Kinoshita, who wore a gray suit that cost more than her annual rent and smelled of expensive tobacco and cheap regret.

She had assumed that relationship was stable.

The envelope suggested otherwise.

She did not open it immediately. Instead, she made tea—green, from a bag, the cheap kind that came in a cardboard box from the supermarket—and sipped it standing at the kitchen counter, watching the envelope as if it might spontaneously combust. The servers hummed in the closet. The AI, Komachi, sent a morning digest to her phone: markets were flat, a Chinese lithium company had missed earnings, and three new medical claims had come in overnight. Two were routine—a broken leg from a construction worker in Kawasaki, a hernia from a former mercenary in Fukuoka. The third was more urgent: a woman in her forties, listed as a “housekeeper” in the fund’s client records, needed surgery for a perforated ulcer. The hospital wanted a ¥400,000 deposit.

Boushi approved the payment before she finished her tea. The money would hit the hospital's account within the hour. She made a note to send a follow-up message to the client—a simple *「お大事に」*—and then she turned back to the envelope.

"Right," she said to the empty room. "Showtime."

She slid a butter knife under the wax seal, careful not to damage the paper. The crimson dragon cracked in two, and she pulled out the contents: a single sheet of high-quality stationery, folded once, and a smaller inner envelope that had been sealed with the same wax.

The letter was brief.


山田某氏殿

貴殿の基金運用におきまして、当組合の貢献度を鑑み、現在の分配率の見直しをお願い申し上げます。

具体的には、年率0.01%分の追加配分を要求いたします。これは当組合の今後の活動資金として不可欠と判断しております。

回答期限：木曜日午後三時、新宿区歌舞伎町二丁目、クラブ「龍宮」にて直接お越しください。

ご不明の点は、以下までご連絡ください。

敬具
龍神会　若頭補佐　元城 銀次

Boushi read the letter three times.

The handwriting was neat, almost elegant—the work of someone who had been taught to write properly, probably in a reform school or a cell. The request was straightforward: a 0.01% increase in the yakuza's share of the fund's annual fee. From 0.01% to 0.02%. From one basis point to two.

She did the math in her head. At current AUM of ¥2.8 trillion, one basis point was ¥280 million per year. An additional basis point would mean another ¥280 million flowing out of the fund, either into the pockets of the yakuza or—more likely—into the pockets of whatever faction within the organization had decided to make a power play.

The fund could technically absorb the increase. The MER was 0.025% total; if she raised the yakuza share to 0.02%, she would be left with 0.005% for her salary and expenses, which was ¥140 million per year. Still more than she needed. Still enough to keep the servers running and the lights on.

But the principle was the problem.

The fund's fee structure was not arbitrary. It was a delicate equilibrium: low enough to attract clients from the legitimate system, high enough to provide minimal operating costs, and split exactly equally between Boushi and the yakuza to signal that neither side held leverage over the other. Any alteration to that ratio, no matter how small, would be read as weakness. If she gave the yakuza more now, they would ask for more later. And then more. And then, eventually, they would ask for everything.

She had seen this pattern before. In the world of illegal finance, there was no such thing as a one-time request. Every concession created a precedent. Every precedent created an expectation. And every expectation, if unmet, became a threat.

The inner envelope was smaller, and she opened it with the same care. Inside was a single photograph.

It showed her apartment building, taken from the street corner across from the combini. The angle was slightly elevated, as if the photographer had been standing on a platform or in the back of a truck. The time was visible on a digital clock in the corner of the photograph: 3:47 AM. The same time window during which the envelope had been delivered.

The message was clear: *We know where you sleep.*

Boushi stared at the photograph for a long moment. Then she set it down, finished her tea, and rinsed the cup in the sink.

She did not feel fear, exactly. She had made peace with the possibility of violence a long time ago, when she had first accepted money from a yakuza underboss and realized that the arrangement was not a business deal but a hostage situation. The dead man's switch was her guarantee, but it was not a shield. It could not stop a bullet. It could only ensure that, if she died, she would take the whole system down with her.

What she felt was something closer to annoyance.

She had a full day of work ahead—the senior section chief had scheduled a meeting to discuss the "urgent" matter of the department's stapler inventory, which she was reasonably sure was not a euphemism for something more interesting—and now she had to deal with a yakuza power struggle before her afternoon coffee break.

"仕方がない," she muttered, pulling out her phone. She scrolled through her contacts until she found the number labeled "Washi" — a code name she had assigned to the senior yakuza member she had met, Kinoshita. The kanji **鷲** meant "eagle," which she thought suited him: old, sharp-eyed, and capable of sudden violence.

She pressed call.

The phone rang seven times before a voice answered.

"Yes?" The voice was male, middle-aged, with the gravelly texture of a heavy smoker and the careful politeness of a man who had learned to be pleasant even when contemplating murder.

"Kinoshita-san," Boushi said. "This is Yamada. From the fund."

A pause. She could hear the rustle of fabric, the click of a door closing. When Kinoshita spoke again, his voice was lower, the politeness replaced by wariness.

"I wasn't expecting a call from you. Is there a problem?"

"I received a letter this morning," Boushi said. "From a man named Genjo Ginji. Young head assistant, he called himself. He's requesting a meeting at a club in Kabukicho on Thursday. He wants an additional 0.01% of the fund's fee."

The silence that followed was longer than the first. Boushi counted the seconds: one Mississippi, two Mississippi, three—

"Genjo," Kinoshita said finally, and the name came out like a curse. "How did he get your address?"

"I assume someone in your organization gave it to him. Or he found it himself. Either way, he has it. He also left a photograph of my apartment building, taken at 3:47 AM, to emphasize the point."

Kinoshita muttered something in a dialect that Boushi couldn't quite parse—Osaka-ben, maybe, or something rougher—and then said, "Don't go to the meeting."

"I wasn't planning to. But he'll escalate if I don't show. He'll take it as a sign of disrespect, or weakness, or both."

"He's young," Kinoshita said, and there was a weariness in his voice that hadn't been there before. "He doesn't understand how things work. He thinks the fund is just another protection racket. He doesn't see that it's—" He stopped. "He doesn't see what it really is."

"He doesn't need to see it," Boushi said. "He just needs to understand that the fund's survival protects his own retirement."

A beat. "What do you mean?"

"The fund holds ¥240 billion from your organization, Kinoshita-san. That includes Genjo's money. If he tries to shake me down for a larger cut, he's not just threatening me—he's threatening the stability of the entire arrangement. If the fund collapses, the government freezes the assets, or I trigger the dead man's switch—" She let the implication hang. "You all lose everything. Including your retirement."

Kinoshita was quiet for a long time. When he spoke, his voice had a new edge to it.

"You're asking me to handle Genjo."

"I'm asking you to remind him that the fund is not a resource to be plundered. It's a partnership. If he treats it like a tap, he'll drain it dry. And then where will he be?"

"In a very cold cell with a very angry boss," Kinoshita said, and there was a dark note of humor in his voice. "I understand. I'll handle it."

"Thank you. And Kinoshita-san?"

"Yes?"

"Thursday at three. Club Ryugu. I'll be there. But I'll be bringing a witness."

"A witness?"

"A recording device," Boushi said. "The kind that uploads automatically to a server outside Japan. If Genjo tries anything, the footage will be in the hands of the NTA, the National Police Agency, and three major newspapers before the night is over. I'd rather not have to use it. But I will."

Kinoshita made a sound that might have been approval or might have been a grunt of frustration.

"You're a dangerous woman, Yamada-san."

"I'm a tired woman, Kinoshita-san. There's a difference."

She ended the call and set the phone down on the kitchen counter. The photograph of her apartment building stared up at her, and she stared back at it, feeling a strange calm settle over her.

The yakuza had sent a message. She had sent a reply. Now the ball was in their court.

Work that day was a blur of spreadsheets and tea. The senior section chief, a man named Mr. Tanaka (not to be confused with the doctor from her childhood, though the name sent a small jolt through her every time she heard it), had indeed called a meeting about the stationery budget, which turned out to be an excuse to complain about the price of Post-it notes and to ask Boushi to "look into" whether they could buy them in bulk from a discount office supply store in Shinagawa.

"Yes, Tanaka-san," she said, bowing slightly. "I'll handle it."

She had said "I'll handle it" so many times in her life that she had begun to wonder if it would appear on her tombstone. *Yamada Boushi: She Handled It.* Below the epitaph, a small engraving of a pension fund balance sheet.

At lunch, she sat alone in the break room, eating a ¥298 onigiri from the combini and scrolling through the AI's summary of the morning's trades. Komachi had executed ¥1.2 billion in rebalancing overnight, shifting exposure from US large caps to Asian small caps, capturing a small arbitrage opportunity in the Korean won's brief dip against the dollar. The fund's NAV had increased by ¥1.8 billion since close of business yesterday.

Her phone buzzed. A message from an unknown number.

`龍宮。木曜日。ヤクザの取り分は変わらない。それで問題ないか。`

Club Ryugu. Thursday. The yakuza's share does not change. Is there a problem?

Boushi typed back: `問題ない。`

No problem.

She put the phone away and finished her onigiri. The rice was slightly dry, the filling slightly bland, but she ate every grain because throwing away food was a sin she could not afford, in budget or in conscience.

Thursday arrived faster than she expected.

She took the afternoon off from work, claiming a doctor's appointment, and changed into the cheapest clothes she owned that still passed for "presentable in a nightclub": a black blouse from a secondhand shop in Shimokitazawa, a gray skirt that had once belonged to a uniform, and a pair of flats that had been resoled three times. She looked, she thought, like a woman who had no money and no power, which was exactly the impression she wanted to give.

The club was in the heart of Kabukicho, a narrow building wedged between a love hotel and a pachinko parlor. The sign above the door was a neon dragon in gold and red, its eyes flickering as if alive. The door was black, windowless, and locked.

She knocked.

A small hatch slid open, revealing a pair of eyes that scanned her face, her clothes, her posture. A voice said, "Name?"

"Yamada Boushi. I'm here to see Genjo-san."

The hatch slid closed. The door clicked open.

The interior of Club Ryugu was a study in contradictions: ostentatious and tasteful, modern and traditional, designed to impress and intimidate in equal measure. The walls were lacquered black wood, the floor was polished concrete, and the lighting was dim red, casting long shadows that moved like living things. A bar stretched across the back wall, lined with bottles of whiskey and shochu that Boushi guessed cost more than her monthly rent combined.

A young man in a black suit approached her. He had the sharp features and dead eyes of someone who had been in the life since he was old enough to hold a weapon. His haircut was expensive. His smile was not.

"Yamada-san," he said, bowing just enough to be technically polite. "Genjo-san is waiting for you. This way."

She followed him through a curtained doorway into a private room. The room was small, dominated by a low table surrounded by four floor cushions. On one cushion sat a man in his early forties, with a shaved head, a diamond-patterned shirt open at the collar, and a gold necklace that spelled his name in kanji: **元城**.

Genjo Ginji. Young head assistant of the Ryujin-kai.

He did not stand when she entered. He did not bow. He gestured to the cushion opposite him with a hand that bore a single, heavy ring on the index finger—a dragon's head cast in gold, its eyes set with tiny emeralds.

"Sit."

Boushi sat. She placed her bag on the floor beside her, close to her leg, and folded her hands in her lap. The bag contained a small recording device, a backup battery, and a collapsible umbrella. She had considered bringing a weapon and decided against it; if Genjo wanted to hurt her, a ¥2,000 pepper spray from Amazon would only make him angry.

"Thank you for coming," Genjo said, and his voice was exactly what she had expected: smooth, confident, and utterly insincere. "I hope my letter was clear."

"It was very clear," Boushi said. "I also received a follow-up message from Kinoshita-san. He indicated that the terms would remain unchanged."

Genjo's eyes flickered—just for a moment, just a flash of something that might have been anger or might have been calculation—and then he smiled.

"Kinoshita is an old man. He doesn't understand the new generation. The fund is growing. Our costs are growing. It's only fair that our share grows with them."

"The fund's fee structure is designed to be stable," Boushi said. "If I increase your share, I have to decrease mine. Or I have to increase the fee on the clients. The first option would make it impossible for me to keep the fund running. The second option would drive clients away. Either way, the fund shrinks. And a smaller fund is worth less to everyone."

Genjo leaned back, studying her with the careful attention of a predator assessing prey.

"You think you understand our business," he said. "You don't. You're a clerk in a cheap apartment. You play with numbers. We play with lives."

"I also play with lives," Boushi said, and she kept her voice level, her gaze steady. "Every day. The difference is that I'm trying to save mine, not take them."

The room went very quiet. The young man who had escorted her in shifted his weight, hand moving toward his jacket. Genjo held up a hand, stopping him.

"Interesting," Genjo said. "You have nerve. I'll give you that." He reached into his jacket and pulled out a small envelope, identical to the one that had been slipped under her door. He tossed it onto the table. It landed with a soft thump.

"A revised offer," he said. "The terms are generous. I suggest you accept them."

Boushi looked at the envelope. She did not touch it.

"Let me explain something to you, Genjo-san," she said. "The fund you're trying to extract more money from? It takes clients that no one else will touch. Former spies. Illegal workers. People who have been so thoroughly discarded by society that they have no legal identity at all. If the fund collapses, they lose everything. And when the fund collapses—because that's what will happen if you keep pulling at this thread—I have a dead man's switch that will release their names, your names, and the entire financial history of this arrangement to every newspaper in the country."

Genjo's smile did not waver, but something in his posture tightened.

"You're threatening me."

"I'm explaining the consequences of your actions. There's a difference." She reached into her bag and pulled out the photograph he had sent her—the picture of her apartment building, taken at 3:47 AM. She laid it on the table, turning it so he could see it.

"You know where I sleep. I know where you sleep. I know where Kinoshita sleeps. I know where the oyabun of the Ryujin-kai sleeps. And I know exactly how much money each of you has in my fund, because I review every single transaction personally." She met his eyes. "You have ¥37 million invested. That's your retirement, Genjo-san. Your exit plan. The only thing standing between you and a cold cell in Fuchu Prison when the oyabun decides you're more trouble than you're worth."

For the first time, Genjo's composure cracked. Just a fraction. Just a flicker of uncertainty in the otherwise unbroken mask.

"How do you know—"

"I'm a clerk in a cheap apartment," she said, and now she let a small, cold smile touch her lips. "I keep the spreadsheets. And I pay attention."

The silence stretched. The young man behind her had stopped breathing, waiting for a signal that did not come. Genjo stared at her, and she stared back, and the space between them was filled with the weight of ¥2.8 trillion and the ghosts of everyone who had ever died because they couldn't afford to live.

Finally, Genjo laughed.

It was not a pleasant laugh—it was harsh, short, and forced—but it broke the tension like a hammer through glass.

"Alright," he said, reaching for the envelope and sliding it back into his jacket. "Alright. You've made your point. The terms stay as they are." He stood, and Boushi stood with him. "But understand this, Yamada-san. This conversation is not over. It's just postponed."

"I understand," Boushi said.

She bowed—a small bow, barely formal, just enough to acknowledge that she had heard him—and turned to leave. The young man in the black suit opened the curtain for her, and she walked out of the private room, through the red-lit bar, and into the gray afternoon light of Kabukicho.

She did not stop walking until she was three blocks away, standing in a convenience store parking lot, her hands shaking so badly that she had to brace them against a vending machine.

"仕方がない," she whispered to herself, the words coming out in a breathless rush. "Someone has to do it."

She bought a bottle of cold tea from the vending machine—¥150, the expensive kind—and drank it standing in the parking lot, watching the neon dragon flicker in the distance.

The fund was safe. For now.

But Genjo's last words echoed in her head, a promise that she knew he would keep.

*This conversation is not over. It's just postponed.*

↑ Back to top

Chapter 3: A Whisper at Blackrock

The Bloomberg terminal blinked at 3:47 AM Tokyo time, which was 2:47 PM in New York, and Michael Chen was still in the office.

This was not unusual. Michael was twenty-nine, a senior analyst in BlackRock's Global Equity Arbitrage desk, and he had not taken a vacation in fourteen months. His apartment in Midtown had a bed he used for sleeping, a kitchen he used for reheating, and a cat he paid a neighbor to feed. The cat's name was Alpha, and it had cost him ¥200,000 from a breeder in Shibuya before he moved to New York. He had brought it on the plane as carry-on.

He was staring at a dataset he had generated himself—a custom screen that ranked all actively managed equity funds by a composite score of risk-adjusted return, volatility, and expense ratio. The screen was designed to catch outliers: funds that were too good to be true. Funds that might be cooking the books, or running a Madoff-style scheme, or doing something else that would make Michael's bonus look very, very good when he reported it to his managing director.

The fund at the top of the list was named, in the database, as "Kabushiki Kaisha Boushi" — a Japanese registered entity with no English name, no website, no marketing contacts, and no presence on any conference circuit that Michael had ever heard of.

The numbers made him blink.

**1-Year Return:** +18.4% vs. VT's +16.2%
**3-Year Annualized:** +14.7% vs. VT's +12.1%
**5-Year Annualized:** +12.9% vs. VT's +10.8%
**Volatility (3-year):** 12.1% vs. VT's 15.3%
**Max Drawdown (5-year):** -8.7% vs. VT's -14.2%
**Expense Ratio:** 0.025% vs. VT's 0.03% (for the institutional share class)
**AUM:** ¥2.8 trillion (approximately $19.2 billion USD)

Michael leaned back in his Aeron chair and rubbed his eyes. The numbers were so clean they looked fake. It was like looking at a car that got 100 miles per gallon, went from zero to sixty in three seconds, and cost ten thousand dollars. If it existed, someone would be selling it for a lot more.

He pulled up the fund's regulatory filings. There were three: a registration statement with Japan's Financial Services Agency, a tax filing with the National Tax Agency, and—oddly—a letter from the Tokyo District Court acknowledging the fund's existence without classifying it as an investment trust, a partnership, or any standard vehicle. The registration was minimal. The fund was classified as a "business entity providing pension-like services to individuals," which was not a category Michael had ever seen.

He scrolled through the holdings disclosures, which were required annually under Japanese law. The fund reported holding 15,247 individual equities, none exceeding 0.02% of total AUM. The geographic breakdown was: Japan 12%, US 18%, Europe 22%, Emerging Markets 48%. The sector breakdown was equally diversified: it looked like someone had taken a chainsaw to the entire investable universe and owned the pieces.

No derivatives. No leverage. No currency hedging. No alternative investments. Just stocks, thousands and thousands of stocks, held in tiny fractions of a percent each.

Michael pulled up VT—the Vanguard Total World Stock Index ETF—and compared the holdings. VT held about 9,500 stocks. This fund held 15,247. That alone explained part of the outperformance: more stocks meant more exposure to small-cap and micro-cap premiums, which historically added 1–2% annualized over the broad index. But the volatility was lower, not higher. That was the anomaly.

He ran a regression. The fund's beta to the MSCI All-World Index was 0.89. Its alpha was +2.3% annually, statistically significant at the 99% confidence level.

"Jesus," he muttered. "Who the hell are you?"

He clicked on the fund's management details. The filing listed exactly one employee: Yamada Boushi, age 26, title "Representative Director," address in Suginami-ku, Tokyo. Salary: ¥2.5 million per year. The fund's registered address was the same apartment building.

Michael laughed out loud.

The office was mostly empty at this hour—just a few analysts from the Japan desk on the other side of the floor, heads down in their own screens—but the laugh echoed anyway. He pulled up Google Maps and found the address: a small apartment building wedged between a pachinko parlor and a love hotel. The street view showed a bicycle chained to a railing, a vending machine, and a cat sleeping on a mailbox.

This was the headquarters of a ¥2.8 trillion pension fund.

He was either looking at the most brilliant piece of financial engineering he had ever seen, or he was looking at the most elaborate fraud since Enron. Either way, it was worth a conversation.

He saved the file, closed the terminal, and stood up. His neck cracked. His wrist ached from mousing. He checked his watch: 4:02 AM Tokyo time, which meant it was still a reasonable hour for compliance in New York. He pulled out his personal phone—not the work one, because he wasn't stupid—and called a number he had saved under "Paulie" in his contacts.

Paul answered on the third ring. "Chen. It's late. Or early. Depending on where you are."

"I'm in Tokyo. It's 4 AM."

"Then it's late. What do you want?"

"I found something. A fund. Unregistered in any major market. ¥2.8 trillion AUM. Expense ratio of two and a half basis points. Returns that beat VT by two hundred basis points annualized with lower volatility."

A pause. "And?"

"And I want to know if compliance has flagged it. Because if they haven't, they need to."

"You're going to throw a Japanese fund under the bus? That's not your lane, Chen. You're on the arbitrage desk."

"The fund's not doing anything illegal, as far as I can tell. It's just... abnormal. The structure is weird. The holdings are weird. The manager is a twenty-six-year-old woman running it out of a closet in Suginami."

Another pause, longer this time. "A closet?"

"The address is an apartment. One employee. No institutional investors. No compliance department. No external auditors listed. It's a ghost."

"Ghosts don't have ¥2.8 trillion," Paul said. "They have offshore bank accounts and shell companies. This sounds like a real thing that's just... small."

"It's not small. It's enormous. And it's invisible. That's the problem."

Paul sighed. "Send me the data. I'll run it through the compliance screen. But Chen—"

"Yeah?"

"Be careful. You're poking into a fund that's probably connected to some very powerful people. The yakuza, the triads, the North Korean embassy—anyone who has money they don't want the government to see. If you flag this thing and it turns out to be a protected asset, you're going to make enemies."

"I'm a BlackRock analyst," Michael said. "I'm already everyone's enemy."

"You're an analyst in Tokyo covering Japanese small caps. You're not Jason Bourne. Keep your head down."

"I'll keep my head down. But I'm sending the data."

"Fine. Send it. But don't expect anything to happen fast. Compliance moves at the speed of bureaucracy."

Michael ended the call and stared at his reflection in the black mirror of the Bloomberg terminal. He could see the tired lines around his eyes, the slight hunch of his shoulders, the gray pallor of a man who had spent too many nights in this chair.

Something about this fund bothered him. It wasn't just the returns. It was the elegance. The structure was so simple, so clean, that it felt almost moral. And morality in finance was usually a sign that you were missing something.

He pulled up the fund's holdings list again and sorted by size. The top holding was ¥3.2 billion—about 0.1% of AUM. In a normal fund, the top ten holdings might be 20% of the portfolio. Here, the top ten were barely 1%. The fund was diversified to a degree that was almost pathological.

He clicked into the fund's stated investment objective, filed with the FSA:

*"To provide retirement and emergency medical income to individuals who are otherwise unable to access the formal financial system, operating at the lowest possible cost to maximize client returns."*

Below that, in smaller print:

*"This fund is not a pension fund. It is a human right."*

Michael stared at the screen for a long time.

"Shit," he said quietly. "She might actually be a good guy."

He sent the data to Paul anyway. Good guys could still be investigated. And if the fund was legit, it would survive scrutiny. If it wasn't, it needed to be shut down.

He saved the file, shut down the terminal, and grabbed his coat. The cat—Alpha—was waiting at home, and he had a dinner reservation he had already canceled twice.

As he walked toward the elevator, he glanced back at the Bloomberg. The screen saver had kicked in, cycling through the day's headlines. He caught a glimpse of a Japanese political news story: *"Diet Committee Discusses Social Security Reform Amid Pressure from US Regulators"*

He would read it tomorrow. Right now, he needed sleep.

The elevator doors closed, and the Tokyo skyline blurred past. Michael pulled out his phone and sent a text to Paul:

`Sent. File name: jp_fund_anomaly_v3.xlsx. Look at the medical sub-portfolio. That's where it gets interesting.`

Then another text:

`Also, check the dead man's switch. I found a reference in the tax filing.`

He pocketed the phone and stared at the ceiling.

Somewhere in Suginami, a woman was probably cooking dinner on a hot plate, watching her servers hum, unaware that a BlackRock analyst had just flagged her fund for a compliance review that would eventually land on the desk of the SEC, the NTA, and God knows who else.

The whisper had begun.

## Continuity Notes

- **New POV introduced:** Michael Chen, 29, BlackRock senior analyst in Tokyo, Global Equity Arbitrage desk. He is tired, driven, and has a cat named Alpha.
- **Fund's data analyzed:** Michael's screen identifies Kabushiki Kaisha Boushi as an extreme outlier in risk-adjusted returns, low volatility, and ultra-low expense ratio. He notes the fund holds 15,247 stocks, no derivatives/leverage.
- **Initial reaction:** Michael is both impressed and suspicious. He contacts a compliance contact ("Paul") and sends the data for review. No action yet—just monitoring flagged.
- **Fund's stated objective revealed:** The FSA filing includes the line "This fund is not a pension fund. It is a human right." — Michael recognizes this as morally charged but doesn't act beyond flagging.
- **Hint of larger implications:** Michael notes the dead man's switch reference in the tax filing and mentions it to Paul. The seed of the SEC investigation (which begins after anonymous tip possibly from BlackRock) is planted.
- **Tone:** Satirical workplace (BlackRock analyst's all-nighter), light thriller (the anomaly discovery), with heart (Michael's reluctant respect for the fund's mission).
- **Timeline:** This chapter takes place during the same period as Chapter 2 (the yakuza confrontation). Boushi is not yet aware of BlackRock's interest.
