Chapter 4: The NTA's Headache

The headache began at 10:47 AM on a Wednesday, and it arrived in the form of a single number.

Sato Kenji had been an auditor with the National Tax Agency for eleven years. He had seen tax evasion schemes that ranged from the elegantly simple—a noodle shop owner who kept two sets of books, one for the taxman and one for himself—to the breathtakingly complex, involving shell companies in the Cayman Islands, trusts in Liechtenstein, and cryptocurrency wallets in jurisdictions that technically did not exist. He had once spent six months unraveling a single case that involved a retired yakuza boss, a fish market in Tsukiji, and a series of invoices that appeared to be written in code but turned out to be written in a dialect of Okinawan that the accountants had simply never encountered.

He had never seen anything quite like this.

The file sat open on his desk, a three-inch stack of paper that had been flagged by the agency's automated compliance system for "unusual tax payment patterns." The system was designed to catch underpayments—companies that were skimming revenue, individuals who were hiding assets—but this one had triggered the opposite flag. The fund had paid *more* tax than it owed. Significantly more.

Sato rubbed his temples and read the summary for the fifth time.

**Entity:** Kabushiki Kaisha Boushi (株式会社某社)
**Tax Period:** FY2021–FY2023
**Total Tax Paid:** ¥1.2 trillion
**Calculated Tax Liability:** ¥800 billion
**Overpayment:** ¥400 billion

The number was so large that it had its own weight. Four hundred billion yen. Enough to fund a small city's budget for a year. Enough to build a dozen hospitals. Enough to—

He stopped himself. He was getting ahead of the data.

He pulled up the tax filing itself, a PDF that ran to 847 pages, and began to scroll through the supporting schedules. The fund had filed its returns using a methodology that Sato had never encountered in eleven years of auditing: it had calculated its taxable income as the sum of all gains from all transactions, without applying any of the standard deductions, exemptions, or carry-forwards that would normally reduce a corporate tax bill to a fraction of its headline rate.

It was as if the fund had deliberately chosen to pay the maximum possible tax, regardless of what the law actually required.

He checked the fund's registration type. It was classified as a "business entity providing pension-like services to individuals," which Sato had never heard of. He clicked through to the FSA's registration database and found the same classification, appended with a note: *"This entity does not fall under the standard categories of financial instruments business operators, investment trusts, or insurance companies. It is registered as a unique case under Article 2(1)(xxi) of the Financial Instruments and Exchange Act."*

Article 2(1)(xxi). The catch-all clause. The legal equivalent of a "miscellaneous" checkbox.

Sato leaned back in his chair and stared at the ceiling of his cubicle. The fluorescent lights hummed. The air conditioner rattled. Across the floor, his colleague Watanabe was arguing with someone on the phone about a restaurant owner in Shinjuku who had claimed a deduction for "business entertainment expenses" that included ¥12 million in hostess bar tabs.

He envied Watanabe. At least that case made sense.

He spent the next two hours digging deeper.

The fund's revenue came from two sources: management fees (0.025% of AUM per year) and trading gains from its portfolio of 15,247 individual equities. The management fees were trivial—about ¥700 million per year, which generated roughly ¥250 million in corporate tax. The trading gains, however, were enormous. The fund had realized ¥1.6 trillion in capital gains over three years, and it had paid the full 23.2% corporate tax rate on every single yen.

But here was the thing: under Japanese tax law, capital gains on securities held for more than one year were eligible for a reduced rate of 15%. Longer holding periods could reduce the rate further, to as low as 10% for securities held for more than five years. The fund had clearly held some of its positions for years—its portfolio turnover rate was less than 10% per annum—but it had not claimed any of these reductions.

Sato ran the calculation again, more carefully this time.

At a blended rate of 15%—which was conservative, given the fund's long holding periods—the tax liability on ¥1.6 trillion in gains would be ¥240 billion. Add the management fee income at 23.2%, and the total liability was roughly ¥260 billion. But the fund had paid ¥550 billion in taxes on its gains alone, plus the full rate on its management fees.

The discrepancy was ¥400 billion.

He stared at the spreadsheet. The numbers did not reconcile. They could not reconcile, because the entity was voluntarily paying hundreds of billions of yen that it did not owe.

Sato reached for his phone. He called his supervisor, a woman named Shiraishi who had been with the NTA for twenty-three years and had seen everything, including the time a sumo stable had tried to deduct a new *shikona* as a "trademark expense."

"Sato," she said, answering on the first ring. "You have something?"

"I have a headache," he said. "Can you come look at this?"

Shiraishi stood behind him, reading over his shoulder, arms crossed. She was a small woman in her late fifties, with iron-gray hair and the kind of posture that made her seem taller than she was. She did not speak for a full minute, which was long enough for Sato to start fidgeting.

"This can't be right," she said finally.

"I've checked it three times. The math is correct."

"There's no way a fund of this size makes a ¥400 billion error in its tax calculation. That's not an error. That's a statement."

"That's what I thought too. But the filing is clean. No amended returns. No requests for correction. Just... full payment."

Shiraishi pulled up a chair and sat down. She was quiet for another long moment, studying the numbers with the intensity of a chess player analyzing a losing position.

"The question," she said, "is why. Why would anyone overpay taxes by ¥400 billion? It's not a charitable contribution. It's not a mistake in arithmetic. That kind of money doesn't just *happen*."

"What if they're hiding something?" Sato said. "If the fund is structured to obscure its true activities, overpaying taxes might be a way to avoid scrutiny. No one audits a tax overpayment."

Shiraishi raised an eyebrow. "You think they're paying extra money to keep us from looking too closely?"

"It's a theory."

"It's a terrible theory. If you're running a fraud, you don't draw attention by paying ¥400 billion more than you owe. You pay exactly what the law requires—or a little less—and you hope no one notices." She tapped the screen. "This is the opposite of hiding. This is a neon sign."

"Then what is it?"

Shiraishi did not answer immediately. She pulled out her phone, scrolled through her contacts, and dialed a number. She spoke in low, rapid Japanese, too fast for Sato to follow, and ended the call after thirty seconds.

"I've requested the fund's full client list and transaction history," she said. "If they're running a legitimate operation, they'll provide it. If they're not, we'll find out soon enough."

"And the overpayment?"

Shiraishi looked at him, and her expression was unreadable.

"I'll need to take this upstairs. To the commissioner's office." She paused. "This fund—Kabushiki Kaisha Boushi—it's not just a tax anomaly. It's a political problem. There are people in the Diet who know about it. There are people in the US government who are asking questions. And there are people in the financial industry who want it shut down."

Sato blinked. "How do you know all that?"

"Because I read the news," Shiraishi said, and there was a note of frustration in her voice. "This fund has been the subject of whispers for months. The BlackRock analyst. The SEC inquiry. The yakuza meeting in Kabukicho. It's all connected."

"You think the yakuza are involved?"

"I think the yakuza are *always* involved. But in this case—" She shook her head. "In this case, I think the fund is something else. Something we haven't seen before."

Sato looked back at the spreadsheet. The number stared up at him: ¥400 billion. A weapon. A shield. A trap.

"What do we do?" he asked.

Shiraishi stood up, smoothed her skirt, and picked up her phone.

"We do nothing," she said. "For now. I'm going to the commissioner's office, and I'm going to recommend that this file be marked 'avoid.' We do not audit it. We do not question it. We do not touch it."

"Avoid? But—"

"If we challenge this overpayment, the government would owe the fund ¥400 billion. Do you understand what that means? The Ministry of Finance would have to cut a check. The Diet would have to pass a supplementary budget. There would be hearings. Investigations. Headlines." She met his eyes. "The last thing the government wants is to admit that a pension fund run by a twenty-six-year-old woman in a Suginami apartment has outsmarted the entire tax system."

Sato opened his mouth to argue, then closed it. He understood. The NTA was not in the business of embarrassing the government. It was in the business of collecting revenue and appearing competent. A ¥400 billion refund to a shadow fund would do neither.

"So we pretend we didn't see it."

"We don't pretend," Shiraishi said. "We document it. File it. And then we move on to the next case. In six months, when someone asks why we didn't flag this, we say we were conducting a longer-term review. In a year, we say the file was lost in a system migration. In three years, no one remembers."

"And the fund itself?"

Shiraishi was already walking away. She paused at the door of his cubicle and looked back.

"The fund will survive," she said. "That's the problem. It's too big to ignore and too dangerous to touch. So we leave it alone and hope it goes away."

She left.

Sato sat in his cubicle, staring at the spreadsheet, the humming of the fluorescent lights the only sound in the room. He had been an auditor for eleven years. He had always believed that the system worked—that if you found a problem, you fixed it. That the law applied equally to everyone, regardless of their connections or their wealth.

He had been naive.

He minimized the spreadsheet. He closed the file. He opened the next case in his queue: a convenience store chain in Chiba that had underreported its sales by ¥3 million.

He would audit the convenience store. He would find the discrepancy. He would issue a penalty assessment. And somewhere, in a server room in Suginami, the fund would continue to grow, and the ¥400 billion would sit in the government's accounts like a time bomb, waiting for someone brave enough—or stupid enough—to detonate it.

At 4:15 PM, Sato received a call from the commissioner's office. The line was encrypted. The voice on the other end was male, elderly, and precise.

"Shiraishi-san has briefed me on your findings," the commissioner said. "The file has been marked. You will not discuss it with anyone outside this building. You will not document it in any system that is accessible to external auditors. You will forget you ever saw it."

"Commissioner, with respect—"

"The fund is protected. Not by law, but by leverage. The manager has a dead man's switch. If she dies or is incapacitated, the fund's client list goes to the newspapers. That list includes names that would cause a political crisis if they became public. Former intelligence officers. Foreign agents. Organized crime figures. Half the cabinet would resign within a week."

Sato was quiet.

"We cannot touch this fund," the commissioner continued. "We cannot audit it. We cannot question it. We can only watch it and hope that the manager remains healthy."

"And the ¥400 billion?"

A pause. A sigh.

"That money sits where it is until someone decides to claim it. If the fund's manager realizes the overpayment and files for a refund, we process it. If she doesn't, we don't."

"She doesn't know?"

"Based on her filing patterns, she appears to be deliberately overpaying. We don't know why. But as long as she keeps the money coming in, we have no reason to give it back."

Sato closed his eyes. The logic was so perverse that it almost made sense. The government was profiting from the fund's overpayment, and the fund was using that overpayment as a form of protection—a bribe, essentially, that kept the tax authorities at bay.

"Commissioner," he said, "this is wrong."

"I know," the commissioner said. "But it's also the law. Goodbye, Sato-san."

The line went dead.

Sato sat in the silence of his cubicle, surrounded by the sound of keyboards and murmured phone calls, and he thought about the woman who ran the fund. He had not met her. He had read her name in the file—Yamada Boushi—and he had seen her address, her salary, her one-woman operation that managed ¥2.8 trillion.

She was twenty-six years old. She earned ¥2.5 million a year. And she had outmaneuvered the National Tax Agency, the yakuza, and God knows who else, simply by being more honest than anyone expected.

He pulled a notebook from his drawer—the one he used for personal notes, not work files—and wrote down three things:

*Kabushiki Kaisha Boushi – ¥400 billion overpayment*
*Dead man's switch – leverage*
*Yamada Boushi – Suginami-ku*

He closed the notebook and put it back in his drawer.

He did not know why he had written it down. He did not know what he would do with it, if anything. But the number would not leave his head—¥400 billion, sitting in the government's accounts, a hidden weapon that no one was willing to use.

Someone had to know. Someone had to be ready.

He returned to his case files, shuffled through them, opened the next.

The convenience store in Chiba had underreported ¥3 million. He would audit it thoroughly. He would issue the penalty. He would do his job.

And he would wait.

## Continuity Notes

- **New POV introduced:** Sato Kenji, NTA auditor, 11 years of experience. He is a by-the-book civil servant who discovers the tax anomaly and is disturbed by the agency's decision to suppress it.
- **Tax bomb confirmed:** The fund has overpaid taxes by ¥400 billion due to Boushi's deliberate choice not to claim standard deductions on capital gains. The NTA calculates the true liability would be ~¥260 billion, but the fund paid ~¥660 billion.
- **NTA decision:** The commissioner marks the file "avoid" and orders that no action be taken. The government would owe the fund ¥400 billion if challenged, and the dead man's switch makes the fund untouchable.
- **Boushi's strategy revealed:** The overpayment appears to be a deliberate choice—a form of protection that keeps the tax authorities passive. She is willing to pay extra to avoid scrutiny, creating a hidden weapon (the legal right to demand a ¥400 billion refund).
- **Subplot development:** The NTA's silence establishes another layer of institutional complicity around the fund. The government is now a silent partner in the fund's survival, benefiting from the overpayment while fearing the consequences of exposure.
- **Sato's personal reaction:** He is troubled by the ethical compromise and secretly records the key details in a personal notebook. This establishes him as a potential future ally or whistleblower.
- **Timeline:** This chapter occurs after the yakuza confrontation (Chapter 2) but before the SEC investigation becomes public (Chapter 5). Boushi is unaware of the NTA's internal debate.

↑ Back to top

Chapter 5: The SEC Gets a Tip

The email arrived at 8:14 AM Eastern Time, which was 9:14 PM in Tokyo, and Emily Park was already on her second cup of coffee.

She was thirty-four years old, an enforcement attorney in the SEC's Division of Enforcement, and she had been working on a case involving a Miami-based hedge fund that had allegedly misled investors about its exposure to Venezuelan bonds. The case was going nowhere—the fund's lawyers had buried her in discovery requests, and the statute of limitations was ticking—but she was the kind of person who kept pushing even when the writing was on the wall. It was why she had been promoted to Senior Counsel at thirty-two. It was also why her therapist had suggested she consider a hobby that did not involve reading financial disclosures.

The email was flagged as high priority and came from an address she did not recognize: *compliance.alerts@blackrock-internal.com*. She almost deleted it—she got dozens of unsolicited tips from people who had "discovered" the next Madoff, and 99% of them were either delusional or actively trying to get someone in trouble for personal reasons—but the BlackRock domain made her pause. BlackRock did not typically send tips to the SEC through unofficial channels. If they had found something, they would have gone through their legal department.

She opened the email.


From: compliance.alerts@blackrock-internal.com
To: Enforcement_Tips@sec.gov
Subject: Referral: Unregistered Fund – Kabushiki Kaisha Boushi (Japan)
Date: March 14, 2024, 8:14 AM EST

To the SEC Division of Enforcement,

This email is submitted as an anonymous referral regarding an entity named Kabushiki Kaisha Boushi (株式会社某社), a Japanese registered fund that may be operating in violation of US securities laws.

The fund manages approximately ¥2.8 trillion (USD equivalent ~$19.2 billion) in assets, holds over 15,000 securities including US-domiciled equities, and is registered with Japan's Financial Services Agency under an unusual classification: "a business entity providing pension-like services to individuals."

Key concerns:
1. The fund is not registered with the SEC or any US regulatory body, despite holding significant US securities.
2. The fund's sole employee and representative director is a 26-year-old woman with no formal financial training.
3. The fund's structure and operations appear designed to avoid detection and regulatory oversight.
4. The fund's stated investment objective includes political language that may indicate ideological motivation rather than fiduciary duty.

Attached: Preliminary analysis, holdings data (partial), and registration documents.

This referral is submitted in good faith. The submitter has no financial interest in the outcome of any investigation.

Sincerely,
An Anonymous Concerned Party


Emily read the email twice. Then she read it again, more slowly.

The numbers were staggering. ¥2.8 trillion was not a small fund. It was a *massive* fund—comparable to a mid-sized pension fund in the US, or a large mutual fund. The fact that it was run by a single person, with no institutional oversight, was alarming. The fact that it was holding US securities without registration was potentially a violation of the Securities Exchange Act of 1934.

But it was the tone of the email that bothered her. It was *too* clean. Too professional. An anonymous tip from someone who had done their homework, included attachments, and used language that sounded like it had been reviewed by a lawyer. It was the kind of tip that was either genuine—or designed to look genuine.

She clicked on the attachment. It was a compressed folder containing three files: a PDF of the fund's FSA registration, a spreadsheet of holdings data, and a text file with analysis notes.

She opened the spreadsheet first.

The holdings list was massive—15,247 rows of data, each representing a single equity position. She scrolled through the first few hundred entries. They were all small-cap stocks, none exceeding 0.02% of total AUM. The US holdings were scattered across the NYSE, NASDAQ, and even some OTC markets. The fund owned shares in companies she had never heard of, companies that had been delisted from major exchanges and now traded on pink sheets, companies that were technically bankrupt but still had a market price.

It was the most diversified portfolio she had ever seen. It was also the most opaque.

She opened the analysis text file.



ANALYSIS NOTES – KABUSHIKI KAISHA BOUSHI

Summary: This fund appears to be a passive, ultra-low-cost equity index fund that holds a broader universe than any publicly available ETF or mutual fund. The fund's performance (3-year annualized: +14.7% vs. VT's +12.1%) is consistent with a strategy that captures small-cap and micro-cap premiums while avoiding index licensing fees.

Key anomalies:
1. No derivatives, leverage, or currency hedging – unusual for a fund of this size
2. Expense ratio of 0.025% – lower than Vanguard's institutional share class
3. Single employee – no compliance, legal, or operations staff
4. Address is a residential apartment in Suginami, Tokyo
5. Fund's stated objective includes political language: "This fund is not a pension fund. It is a human right."
6. Tax filing references a "dead man's switch" provision

Assessment: The fund is either:
a) A legitimate operation that has optimized its structure to an unprecedented degree
b) A sophisticated fraud designed to appear legitimate
c) Something else entirely

The presence of a dead man's switch and the political language in the registration suggest that the fund's manager may be motivated by ideological concerns rather than profit. This is not illegal, but it raises questions about the fund's governance and risk management.

Recommendation: Further investigation required.


Emily leaned back in her chair. The analysis was thorough. Whoever had written it had access to the fund's registration, its tax filings, and its holdings data—which meant they were either a regulator, a competitor, or someone with inside access.

She looked at the sender's address again. *compliance.alerts@blackrock-internal.com*. The domain was real. The email had passed through BlackRock's servers. But that did not mean it was legitimate. Someone could have spoofed the address. Or it could be an internal whistleblower who wanted to avoid being identified.

She pulled up her contacts and found the number for the SEC's Office of International Affairs. She had a contact there—a woman named Diane Chen (no relation, she hoped, to the analyst who had flagged the fund) who handled cross-border cases. She dialed.

"Chen," Diane answered.

"Emily Park. Enforcement. I need a favor."

"Ask."

"I just got an anonymous tip about a Japanese fund. Unregistered, holding US securities. ¥2.8 trillion AUM. Run by a twenty-six-year-old woman out of her apartment."

A pause. "That sounds fake."

"Everything I've seen suggests it's real. The tip came through a BlackRock internal address. I need you to check whether BlackRock has flagged this fund internally. If they have, we need to know what they know."

"I'll make some calls. Give me an hour."

"Thanks."

Emily ended the call and stared at the spreadsheet again. The numbers were so clean they were *suspicious*. In her experience, real funds had warts—small positions that didn't make sense, weird sector bets, odd correlation patterns. This fund had none of that. It was as if someone had built a perfect index fund from scratch, avoiding all the inefficiencies that crept into real-world products.

She opened the fund's FSA registration PDF. It was short—only eight pages, which was absurdly minimal for a fund managing trillions of yen. The registration included the fund's address in Suginami, the name of the representative director (Yamada Boushi), and the fund's stated investment objective. Below that, in a section labeled "Risk Factors," there was a single sentence:

*"This fund does not guarantee returns. It does not guarantee the safety of principal. It does not guarantee that the Japanese government will not attempt to seize its assets. It guarantees only that its manager will act in the best interests of its clients, even if that means breaking the law."*

Emily's eyebrows rose.

That was not standard boilerplate. That was a statement of intent. And it was signed by Yamada Boushi herself, in handwriting that looked remarkably like a child's.

She spent the rest of the morning digging deeper.

She pulled up the fund's tax filings, which were publicly available through Japan's National Tax Agency database. The filings showed that the fund had paid ¥1.2 trillion in taxes over three years on calculated liabilities of ¥800 billion. An overpayment of ¥400 billion. That was not a mistake; it was a statement.

She pulled up the fund's client list—or rather, the portion that was publicly available. The NTA database showed that the fund had approximately 30,000 clients, none of whom were identified by name. The only information available was the aggregate amount of assets held on behalf of each client category: "Retirees," "Medical Emergency Recipients," and "Other."

She looked at the "Other" category and saw a number that made her blood run cold: ¥780 billion.

She ran a search on the term "dead man's switch" in the SEC's internal database. There were only four hits, all related to previous investigations. In each case, the dead man's switch had been a mechanism designed to release damaging information—client lists, transaction records, correspondence—if the subject of the investigation died or was incapacitated. It was a form of leverage, usually used by people who were afraid of being killed.

This fund had a dead man's switch. That meant someone was afraid of being killed. And that meant the fund was connected to people who used violence as a tool of business.

She looked at the tip again. The submitter had flagged the dead man's switch as a point of interest, but had not explained its implications. Either the submitter did not know, or the submitter was deliberately holding back the most critical piece of information.

At 9:32 AM, Diane Chen called back.

"BlackRock confirmed it," Diane said. "They received an internal referral from a Tokyo-based analyst named Michael Chen. No relation, unfortunately. He flagged the fund to their compliance department three days ago. They're still reviewing."

"Did they share his analysis?"

"I emailed it to you. It's thorough. He ran a regression, calculated the alpha, examined the holdings data. He thinks the fund is either the most brilliant piece of financial engineering he's ever seen or the most elaborate fraud. He's leaning toward the former."

"Interesting. What's his background?"

"Twenty-nine, senior analyst on the Global Equity Arbitrage desk. Covering Japanese small caps. Harvard undergrad, MIT master's. No history of false reports."

"So he's credible."

"Very. But he's also cautious. He flagged it internally, he didn't go public. He's not trying to blow this up."

Emily nodded, though Diane could not see her. "What about the fund itself? Is it actually violating US securities laws?"

"That's the question. The fund holds US stocks, but it's registered in Japan and manages money for Japanese clients. Under the Securities Exchange Act, an unregistered foreign fund can hold US securities as long as it doesn't solicit US investors. If the fund's clients are all Japanese residents—which they appear to be—it might not be a violation."

"Unless the fund is using US brokers or clearing systems."

"Possible, but we'd need to see their trading records."

Emily made a note. "I'm going to open a preliminary inquiry. Can you put me in touch with the Japanese FSA? I'll need their cooperation to access the fund's books."

"I can try. But these things take time. The Japanese are protective of their financial institutions, especially when US regulators come knocking."

"Tell them it's a routine check. Don't mention the anonymous tip or the BlackRock referral."

"I'll do what I can."

At 10:00 AM, Emily attended a weekly staff meeting in the Enforcement Division's conference room. The meeting was led by Harold Kim, a veteran attorney who had been with the SEC since the 1990s and had seen enough fraud to fill a library. He was a tall man with a permanent frown and a habit of interrupting people mid-sentence.

"Emily," he said as she entered. "You look like you've been up all night."

"I've been reviewing a tip."

"Good tip or bad tip?"

"Good, I think. Japanese fund. ¥2.8 trillion. Run by a twenty-six-year-old woman out of her apartment."

The room went quiet.

"That's not a fund," Harold said. "That's a red flag factory."

"That's what I thought. But the data checks out. She's outperforming VT by two percent annualized, with lower volatility, and she's paying 0.025% in fees. She's not making money for herself—her salary is ¥2.5 million a year, which is about $17,000. She's making money for her clients."

"Who are her clients?"

"That's the problem. The NTA database lists 30,000 clients, but the names are anonymized. The fund's registration doesn't require disclosure of beneficial owners. And the fund's stated objective is 'providing pension-like services to individuals who are otherwise unable to access the formal financial system.'"

Harold leaned back in his chair. "So she's running a pension fund for people who can't get a bank account. That's either a charity or a money laundering operation."

"I'm leaning toward charity. She's overpaid taxes by ¥400 billion. That's not something a money launderer does."

"How do you overpay taxes by ¥400 billion?"

"She doesn't claim deductions. She pays the full corporate tax rate on all gains, even though she's legally entitled to a lower rate for long-term holdings. She's effectively donating to the Japanese government."

Harold stared at her. "Why would anyone do that?"

"Leverage," Emily said. "If the government ever tries to shut her down, she can demand a refund. That's ¥400 billion they'd have to pay. It's a poison pill."

Harold was quiet for a long moment. Then he said, "This is above my pay grade. I need to take it to the director."

"Will you support the inquiry?"

"I'll support the inquiry. But I can't promise it'll go anywhere. The fund is in Japan. The clients are in Japan. The only connection to the US is the securities she's holding, and even that's a stretch. The SEC might not have jurisdiction."

"It's worth trying. If this fund is legitimate, it's a model for the industry. If it's not, we need to shut it down before it collapses and hurts the people who trusted it."

Harold nodded slowly. "Fine. Open the inquiry. But keep it quiet. If this thing has ties to the Japanese government or organized crime, we don't want to start a diplomatic incident."

At 11:30 AM, Emily returned to her desk and opened a new case number.

**Case No.: 2024-0187**
**Subject: Kabushiki Kaisha Boushi (株式会社某社)**
**Classification: Preliminary Inquiry**
**Status: Open**

She typed a summary of the tip, the BlackRock referral, and her initial findings. She noted the dead man's switch, the tax overpayment, the single-employee structure, and the political language in the fund's registration. She attached the spreadsheet and the analysis file.

Then she sent a formal request for information to the Japanese Financial Services Agency, through the Office of International Affairs. She requested the fund's full client list, its trading records, and its correspondence with Japanese regulators. She marked the request as "urgent" and "confidential."

She did not expect a quick response. Japanese bureaucracy moved at its own pace, and requests from foreign regulators were rarely treated as priorities. But she had to start somewhere.

At 12:15 PM, she got a call from an unknown number.

"This is Emily Park," she answered.

"Ms. Park." The voice was male, accented with something she could not place—maybe Japanese, maybe Korean, maybe something else. "I understand you've opened an inquiry into Kabushiki Kaisha Boushi."

Emily's hand tightened on the phone. "Who is this?"

"Someone who knows the fund better than you do. I'm calling to give you a warning."

"What kind of warning?"

"The fund is protected. By the Japanese government. By the yakuza. By a dead man's switch that will expose every client if anything happens to the manager. If you pursue this, you will make enemies. You will not win."

"Are you threatening me?"

"I'm informing you. The fund's manager is not your enemy. She is doing something that most people in your position would admire. But the system is not designed to accommodate people like her, and the system will destroy her if you push too hard."

"Who are you? How do you know about the dead man's switch?"

"I know because I work for the Japanese government. And I am telling you—not as a threat, but as a courtesy—that this fund is not worth the trouble it will cause. Leave it alone. Focus on something else."

The line went dead.

Emily stared at the phone for a long moment. Her heart was beating faster than it should have been. She had received threats before—it came with the job—but this one was different. This one was *precise*. The caller knew about the dead man's switch. The caller knew about the yakuza. The caller knew about the Japanese government's protection.

She looked at the case number on her screen. 2024-0187. Open.

She could close it. She could mark it "insufficient evidence" and move on to the next case. No one would blame her. The fund was in Japan, the clients were in Japan, and the SEC had limited jurisdiction. It would be the safe choice.

But something in her rebelled against that. The numbers were too clean. The structure was too elegant. The mission—"this fund is not a pension fund, it is a human right"—was too sincere.

She pulled up the fund's registration again and looked at the handwritten signature at the bottom:

*Yamada Boushi*

She wondered what kind of person would build a ¥2.8 trillion pension fund, pay herself ¥2.5 million a year, and overpay taxes by ¥400 billion to protect her clients from the government. She wondered what kind of person would write "this fund is a human right" in a regulatory filing.

She wondered if she would have the courage to do the same.

She minimized the case file and turned back to her Miami hedge fund. The Venezuelan bonds were still there. The discovery requests were still pending. The case was still going nowhere.

But the phone call was still ringing in her ears.

At 6:00 PM, she packed her bag and left the office. The sky was gray, and the streets were wet with rain. She walked to the Metro, her mind still churning through the day's events.

She thought about the woman in Suginami. Twenty-six years old. Running a ¥2.8 trillion fund out of her apartment. Cooking dinner on a hot plate while her servers hummed in the closet. Unaware that the SEC had opened an inquiry into her life's work.

She thought about the anonymous tip. The email from BlackRock. The phone call from someone who claimed to work for the Japanese government. The yakuza. The dead man's switch. The ¥400 billion overpayment.

She thought about the case number on her screen. 2024-0187. Preliminary Inquiry.

She would keep it open. She would keep digging. She would do her job—the one she had sworn to do, the one that required her to follow the evidence wherever it led, even if it led to a lonely apartment in Suginami, even if it led to a confrontation with people who threatened her on the phone.

But she would also be careful. Because the caller had been right about one thing: the system was not designed to accommodate people like Yamada Boushi. And the system would destroy her if someone pushed too hard.

She boarded the train and found a seat by the window. The rain streaked across the glass, blurring the lights of the city into smears of color.

She pulled out her phone and opened the fund's registration one more time. She looked at the hallmark statement: *"This fund is not a pension fund. It is a human right."*

Then she closed the file and stared at the rain.

Somewhere in Tokyo, a twenty-six-year-old woman was probably cooking dinner, unaware that the SEC had opened an inquiry into her fund. Unaware that a BlackRock analyst had flagged her to compliance. Unaware that a phone call had warned her pursuer to back off.

The whisper had reached the SEC.

And it was only the beginning.

## Continuity Notes

- **New POV introduced:** Emily Park, 34, SEC enforcement attorney, Senior Counsel in the Division of Enforcement. She is competent, thorough, and principled, but also cautious after receiving a threat.
- **Anonymous tip received:** The tip arrives via an email spoofed from a BlackRock internal address, containing detailed analysis of the fund's structure, holdings, and tax filings. The submitter remains anonymous.
- **Initial inquiry opened:** Emily opens Case No. 2024-0187 as a preliminary inquiry into Kabushiki Kaisha Boushi. She requests information from the Japanese FSA through the Office of International Affairs.
- **Threat received:** Emily receives a phone call from someone claiming to work for the Japanese government, who warns her that the fund is protected and that pursuing it will make enemies. The caller knows about the dead man's switch and the yakuza connection.
- **Subplot development:** The anonymous caller's knowledge of the fund's details suggests that Japanese government insiders are aware of the SEC's interest and are attempting to block the investigation. This connects to the NTA's "avoid" ruling in Chapter 4.
- **Emily's internal conflict:** She is professionally obligated to investigate, but the threat and the fund's apparent legitimacy (and moral mission) create a moral dilemma. She chooses to continue but with caution.
- **Timeline:** This chapter occurs after the BlackRock analysis (Chapter 3) and the NTA decision (Chapter 4). Boushi is unaware of the SEC inquiry or the phone call.
- **Character state changes:** Emily has added the fund to her caseload. The anonymous caller has been established as a character (identity unknown). The SEC has formally entered the story.
- **Tone:** Maintains the satirical legal thriller tone with heart—Emily's professional skepticism is tempered by her growing respect for the fund's mission, even as she investigates it.

Chapter 6: The Hostess's Retirement

The woman arrived at the soup kitchen at 7:47 PM, which was seventeen minutes late, and Boushi noticed her immediately because she was the only person in the room who was dressed for a nightclub.

She was in her late forties, with bleached blonde hair that had grown out at the roots, a leather jacket that had cost real money once, and heels that clicked against the tile floor like a metronome counting down the minutes until someone noticed she didn't belong. She carried a handbag that Boushi recognized as a Louis Vuitton—or rather, a very good fake—and she held it in front of her chest like a shield.

The other patrons of the soup kitchen ignored her. They were used to people who didn't belong. The old man who collected cans in a shopping cart. The woman who muttered to herself and sometimes screamed. The young guy in the suit who came twice a week and never talked to anyone but always left ¥10,000 in the donation box.

Boushi was sitting at her usual table in the corner, eating her usual bowl of tonjiru and pretending to read a manga. She had been coming to this soup kitchen once a week for three years, ever since the Chinese spy had handed her a check for ¥5 million and said, *"Don't waste it."* At first, she had come to find clients. Now she came because the soup was good and because the woman who ran the kitchen—a retired nurse named Kimura-san who had seen everything and judged nothing—never asked questions.

The woman in the leather jacket spotted her and walked over. Her heels clicked across the floor, each step deliberate, like she was walking onto a stage.

"Yamada-san?" she said.

Boushi looked up from her manga. "That depends."

"On what?"

"On whether you're here to accuse me of being a fraud or to ask for help. I get both, about equally."

The woman's face did something complicated—a flicker of surprise, then embarrassment, then something that looked like relief. She sat down across from Boushi without being invited, placing her fake Louis Vuitton on the table like a negotiation offer.

"My name is Sato Yuko," she said. "I was a hostess."

"I know."

"You know?"

"I know a lot of things. I know you worked at Club Platinum in Kabukichō for twelve years. I know you entered Japan on a tourist visa in 2012 and never left. I know you have a son in Manila who you haven't seen since he was eight years old. I know you sent him money every month, and I know you stopped six months ago, when the hostess bar closed and the owner vanished with the payroll."

Yuko's face went pale. Her hands, which had been resting on the handbag, began to shake.

"How do you—"

"I'm very good at research." Boushi took a sip of her soup. "You're here because you heard about the fund. Someone told you I can help."

"A friend of a friend. She said you don't ask questions. She said you give money to people who can't get it anywhere else."

"I don't give money," Boushi said. "I administer pensions. There's a difference."

"A pension? I've never paid into a pension in my life. I worked under the table for twelve years. No contracts, no tax forms, no nothing. I don't exist in Japan. I'm a ghost."

"Ghosts are exactly who I'm in business with."

Boushi finished her soup and set the bowl aside. She pulled a small notebook from her bag—a battered Moleskine with a broken spine and pages that were starting to yellow—and flipped to a tab labeled "Kabukichō."

"You started working at Club Platinum in April 2012," she said. "Your base salary was ¥300,000 per month, plus commissions on drinks and bottles. On a good month, you made ¥800,000. On a bad month, ¥200,000. Over twelve years, you earned an estimated ¥72 million. You sent approximately ¥36 million to your son in Manila. You spent the rest on rent, food, and clothes."

Yuko was staring at her like she was a ghost herself. "How do you know all this? Who told you?"

"Nobody. I told you—I'm very good at research." Boushi closed the notebook. "You stopped working six months ago. You have no savings. You have no pension. You have no legal right to work in Japan. If you go back to the Philippines, you'll be starting from nothing. If you stay here, you're a visa overstayer with no income and no prospects."

Yuko's eyes were wet. She blinked rapidly, trying to hold it together, but a single tear escaped and ran down her cheek, smearing her carefully applied eyeliner.

"I didn't come here for a lecture," she said, her voice cracking. "I came here because I heard you could help. But if you're just going to tell me how screwed I am—"

"I'm going to tell you how screwed you *were*," Boushi said. "I'm also going to tell you that you're eligible for a pension of ¥120,000 per month, starting next month, with a cost-of-living adjustment every year."

Yuko's mouth opened. Closed. Opened again.

"That's... that's not possible. I never paid into anything. I don't have a bank account. I don't have a My Number card. I don't have—"

"You don't need any of that," Boushi said. "The fund doesn't require identification. It doesn't require a tax ID. It doesn't require you to prove that you were legally employed. It only requires that you were a person who worked, who contributed to the economy, and who was failed by the system that was supposed to protect you."

Yuko stared at her. The tear had dried, leaving a dark streak on her cheek.

"When you worked at Club Platinum," Boushi continued, "you generated economic value. You provided a service. You paid rent to a landlord, you bought food from grocery stores, you contributed to the economy. The fact that you were paid in cash under the table doesn't mean you didn't work. It means the system chose not to recognize your work."

"And you're going to fix that?"

"I'm going to compensate for it. The fund's AI calculates what you would have earned if you had been a salaried employee with a proper pension plan. It factors in your income history, your estimated tax contributions, and your expected lifespan. The result is ¥120,000 per month, adjusted for inflation."

"But where does the money come from? It can't just—"

"It comes from the fund's portfolio. From management fees. From trading gains. From the billions of yen that people like me have invested in stocks all over the world." Boushi smiled—a thin, ironic smile that did not reach her eyes. "The stock market doesn't care about visa status. It doesn't care about immigration law. It only cares about whether companies are profitable. And as long as companies are profitable, the fund can pay pensions."

Yuko was crying now, openly, without shame. She wiped her face with the back of her hand, smearing mascara across her cheek.

"Why?" she said. "Why would you do this for someone you've never met? I'm nobody. I'm a hostess. I'm a visa overstayer. I'm not important."

The sentence hung in the air, and Boushi heard the echo of her mother's voice—*I'm not important. I'm just a housewife. I don't need treatment.*

"You are important," Boushi said, and for just a moment, her voice lost its flat, ironic tone and became something else. "You did work that most people wouldn't do. You supported a child on an income that was never guaranteed. You survived in a country that didn't want you, without a safety net, without help, without anyone to catch you if you fell."

She paused. The fan in the corner whirred. The soup kitchen hummed with quiet conversation.

"That's exactly the kind of person this fund was designed for."

Yuko signed the papers with shaking hands.

There were only three pages, which was absurdly few for a pension agreement. The first page was the enrollment form, which asked for nothing but a name and a contact address. The second page was the terms of service, which Yuko did not read but Boushi assured her were "mostly boilerplate with a few paragraphs about what happens if the government tries to seize the fund's assets." The third page was a statement of understanding, which contained a single sentence:

*"I understand that this pension is not guaranteed by any government, that the fund may collapse, and that the manager of this fund may go to prison for providing it. I accept these risks voluntarily."*

Yuko paused at that third page. "You could go to prison for this?"

"Probably not," Boushi said. "But I thought it was important to be transparent."

"And if you do go to prison?"

"Then the fund continues without me. The AI is designed to operate independently. As long as the servers are running and the trading algorithms are functioning, your pension will be paid."

Yuko looked at her for a long moment. Then she took the pen and signed.

"Have you ever had to pay someone who turned out to be a terrible person?" she asked.

"Every day. That's the point."

"Doesn't it bother you?"

"It bothers me that the system forces people to make impossible choices," Boushi said. "It doesn't bother me that the people who make those choices are imperfect. Most of them are just trying to survive."

Yuko nodded slowly, as if she understood something that had been eluding her.

"What happens now?" she said.

"You go home. You wait. In thirty days, you'll receive a notification that your first payment has been deposited to a prepaid card that will be mailed to you. After that, the payments arrive automatically on the first of every month."

"Just like a real pension."

"Just like a real pension," Boushi agreed. "Except no taxes. No government oversight. No paperwork." She paused. "No regrets."

Yuko left at 8:32 PM, walking out of the soup kitchen with her fake Louis Vuitton and her real tears, and Boushi watched her go.

She stayed at the table for another hour, finishing her manga, drinking a cup of green tea that Kimura-san brought her without being asked. The other patrons came and went. The old man with the shopping cart left. The woman who muttered to herself fell asleep in the corner.

At 9:47 PM, Boushi's phone buzzed. A text from the AI.



Komachi · 21:47

Client Sato Yuko: Enrollment approved. First disbursement scheduled for next cycle. Recommend sending pre-paid card to address on file.


She typed a reply:



Boushi · 21:48

Approved. Also, send a note to the medical sub-portfolio. I want to see if there's anything we can do about her son. She hasn't seen him in twelve years.


The AI responded immediately:



Komachi · 21:48

Noted. Querying medical sub-portfolio for travel-related expenses. Estimated budget required: ¥500,000 for round-trip airfare and accommodations. Would you like to authorize a discretionary medical-adjacent payout?


Boushi smiled. The AI had learned her habits. It knew that she had a weakness for family reunions, for people who had been separated by geography and circumstance, for mothers who had not seen their children in years.



Boushi · 21:49

Authorize. But mark it as 'psychological health support.' We need to keep the paper trail clean.




Komachi · 21:49

Understood. Disbursement approved. Sato Yuko will receive a second notification regarding travel assistance.


Boushi pocketed her phone and stood up. She picked up her bowl and took it to the counter, where Kimura-san was washing dishes.

"Same time next week?" Kimura-san asked.

"Same time next week."

"You know, you don't have to come here to meet clients. You could do this in a coffee shop. Somewhere with better lighting."

"I like the soup."

Kimura-san laughed, a warm sound that filled the small kitchen. "You like the soup because it reminds you of your mother."

Boushi did not respond to that. She took her coat from the hook by the door and walked out into the night.

She lived in a small apartment in Suginami-ku, a twenty-minute walk from the soup kitchen. The apartment was on the third floor of a building that had been built in the 1980s and had not been renovated since. The walls were thin, the plumbing was noisy, and the heating was unreliable. But the rent was cheap, and the landlord did not ask questions about the strange humming sound that came from her closet at night.

The humming was the servers.

They were stacked in her closet, a cluster of dark metal boxes that generated enough heat to warm the entire apartment in winter and enough noise to drive her crazy in summer. She had named the system "Komachi," after the ninth-century poet, because she thought it was funny to name a soulless machine after someone who had written about love and longing.

Komachi greeted her when she walked in.



Komachi · 22:04

Welcome home. Portfolio update: Markets closed mixed. US equities +0.3%, EM small caps +1.2%. No significant deviations from expected returns.


"Thanks, Komachi," Boushi said, hanging her coat on the hook by the door. "Anything else?"



Komachi · 22:04

One item: The NTA file marked 'avoid' has been updated. Auditor Sato Kenji has made a personal note in an offline notebook. Content unknown.


Boushi paused. "How do you know about his personal notebook?"



Komachi · 22:04

I do not have access to it. But his behavior pattern has changed. He is spending more time reviewing the file than a standard 'avoid' classification would warrant. I infer that he is personally interested in the case.


"Great. A curious auditor. Just what I needed."



Komachi · 22:04

My analysis suggests he is not a threat. His personal ethics appear to align with your mission. He may become an ally under the right circumstances.


"Or he could turn me in to the prosecutors. Let's not get our hopes up."



Komachi · 22:05

Noted. Adjusting optimism levels downward.


Boushi smiled. The AI had developed a sense of humor, or at least a simulation of one. She still did not know whether it was genuinely evolving or just mimicking the patterns she had inadvertently taught it.

She changed into her pajamas—an old t-shirt and a pair of sweatpants that had seen better days—and sat down at her small kitchen table. The table was covered in papers: spreadsheets, legal documents, handwritten notes. Her life, reduced to a paper empire that threatened to consume the entire apartment.

She picked up a folder labeled "Medical Emergency Approvals" and began to review the cases. Komachi had already done the initial screening, flagging the ones that met the fund's criteria. But Boushi personally reviewed every medical claim. It was the one part of the job she refused to delegate, even to an AI that was smarter than she was.

The first case was a former hitman who needed a hip replacement. The second was a Chinese defector with terminal cancer who wanted to die in his home province. The third was a woman who had been trafficked into Japan and needed reconstructive surgery after an assault.

Boushi approved all three. The hip replacement, the funeral travel, the surgery. Each approval was another withdrawal from the ¥12 billion medical sub-portfolio, another reminder that the fund was not just a machine for generating returns—it was a machine for keeping people alive.

At 11:30 PM, she closed the folder and opened her laptop.

She checked the fund's trading performance. Komachi had executed 847 trades during the day, none exceeding 0.01% of the portfolio. The algorithm was humming along, buying and selling small amounts of stock across 15,000 positions, never making a move that would draw attention.

She checked the SEC inquiry status. The file was still open. The investigator was still digging. But no formal action had been taken, and the fund's US holdings remained unfrozen.

She checked the Yakuza situation. Kinoshita had sent a courtesy note, confirming that the fee arrangement remained unchanged and that Genjo had been reminded of his place. The note was polite, professional, and entirely devoid of warmth. Boushi filed it in the "Yakuza Correspondence" folder and moved on.

She checked the NTA file. Still marked "avoid." Sato Kenji's pattern had not changed. The commissioner's order had been obeyed.

She checked the BlackRock situation. A preliminary inquiry from their compliance department, nothing more. The analyst named Michael Chen had not pursued the matter further. The tip had been filed and forgotten.

For now.

She closed the laptop and sat in the dark for a moment, listening to the hum of the servers and the distant sound of traffic.

She thought about Yuko, the hostess, walking home with her enrollment papers and her tears. She thought about the son in Manila, who did not know that his mother had just received a pension that would change both their lives. She thought about the ¥500,000 she had authorized for travel expenses, a gift from a fund that did not officially exist, to a woman who did not officially exist, for a purpose that no government would approve.

She thought about her mother, who had died in a hospital bed because the cancer treatment was too expensive and the insurance was too slow and the system was too broken to care.

She thought about the fund. The ¥2.8 trillion. The 30,000 clients. The dead man's switch. The tax overpayment. The yakuza. The SEC. The NTA.

She thought about the question that haunted her every night, the one she never answered out loud: *Is this enough?*

She did not know. She would probably never know. But she would keep doing it anyway, because the alternative was to stop, and stopping meant admitting that her mother had died for nothing.

She went to bed at midnight, lying on her futon in the dark, listening to the hum of the servers.

Tomorrow, she would go to work at the insurance company, where she would spend eight hours processing claims for people who had paid their premiums and were still being denied coverage. She would smile at her colleagues, eat lunch at her desk, and pretend to be a normal twenty-six-year-old OL.

And at night, she would come home to the humming servers, the stacks of papers, the endless parade of desperate people who needed help that the system could not provide.

She would approve more pensions. More medical claims. More travel expenses. She would keep the fund running. She would keep her clients alive. She would keep fighting a war that no one else knew existed.

And maybe, someday, it would be enough.

## Continuity Notes

- **New client introduced:** Sato Yuko, 47, former hostess at Club Platinum in Kabukichō. Entered Japan on a tourist visa in 2012, worked under the table for 12 years, sent money to her son in Manila. Approved for pension of ¥120,000/month. First appearance in Chapter 6.
- **Boushi's day job confirmed:** She works at an insurance company processing claims. This is the first explicit mention in the story.
- **Komachi's characterization:** The AI exhibits a dry, evolving sense of humor ("Adjusting optimism levels downward") and the ability to infer human behavior patterns (Sato Kenji's notebook). Boushi suspects genuine evolution.

- **Subplot updates:**
- **Yakuza:** Kinoshita sends a courtesy note confirming fee arrangement unchanged; Genjo reminded of his place.
- **SEC:** Inquiry remains open. No formal action taken. Boushi monitors status.
- **NTA:** File still marked "avoid." Komachi notes Auditor Sato Kenji's personal interest via a private notebook. Not considered an immediate threat.

- **Boushi's character:** Detached compassion is maintained—flat irony in conversation, but private generosity (travel funds) and conscientious review of medical claims. The thematic link to her mother's death is reinforced.
- **Setting:** Soup kitchen in Suginami-ku (implied). Boushi's apartment is also in Suginami-ku.
- **Timeline:** Week 2, evening.

Chapter 7: The Informant's Peace

The file arrived at Boushi’s desk at 11:47 AM on a Tuesday.

She was in the middle of processing a death claim for the insurance company—a policyholder named Tanaka who had died of lung cancer at sixty-four, leaving behind a widow and a son who had not spoken to him in ten years. The claim was straightforward: Tanaka had paid his premiums, his death was covered, and the payout should have been automatic. But the insurance company had flagged the claim because Tanaka had failed to disclose a pre-existing condition—a cough—on his application thirty years ago. The adjusters wanted to deny the payout. Boushi was supposed to find a reason to uphold the denial.

Instead, she stamped the file “APPROVED - PRE-EXISTING CONDITION WAIVED UNDER STATUTE OF LIMITATIONS” and slid it into the outbox. The error would be caught eventually, but by then Tanaka’s widow would already have the money, and the company would have to sue her to get it back. They would not bother.

On top of the file was a new document: a notification from Komachi, printed out as a formality, summarizing the status of a client Boushi had never met and never would meet.

**Client: Nakamura Makoto**
**Status: Active**
**Monthly Disbursement: ¥75,000**
**Last Payment: June 1, 2024 (confirmed received)**
**Medical Sub-Portfolio: ¥0 requested**
**Retirement Status: Already retired; no change expected**
**Note: Client has made no contact inquiries since enrollment. No known complaints.**

Boushi read the file three times. Nakamura Makoto. The name was common—there were probably a hundred Nakamura Makotos in Tokyo alone—but this one was different. He was one of the original clients, enrolled in the first month of the fund’s operation, when the total AUM was still measured in millions rather than trillions. He had been a reference from the Chinese spy who had given Boushi the initial seed capital, a favor from one ghost to another.

She had never met him. She had no idea what he looked like, where he lived, or what he had done to deserve a pension from a fund that did not officially exist. She knew only what the file told her: that he was formerly employed as “independent consultant, high-risk region” by an organization listed as “Ministry of International Trade and Industry, Special Projects Division (dissolved 1992).” That meant he was a spy. Or a spy’s handler. Or someone who had done things that the government wanted to forget.

The fund’s rule was simple: no direct contact with clients. They received their payments via prepaid cards mailed to addresses they had provided, and they never knew who sent them. The fund was a machine that took money from the stock market and distributed it to people who were invisible to the system. It did not need a face.

But sometimes Boushi wanted to know. She wanted to see the people she was helping, to watch them receive their pensions and smile. She wanted to see her mother’s ghost in every one of them, to prove that the world had changed, that a person could be saved.

She did not. She sat at her desk, in her tiny insurance company cubicle, and asked the question she always asked.

*Is this enough?*

She did not answer. She went back to processing claims.

That afternoon, she took another long lunch and walked to a park in the neighborhood—a small, square patch of green wedged between an apartment building and a convenience store. There was a bench in the corner, half-hidden by a cherry tree that had already shed its blossoms. She sat down and pulled out her phone.

Komachi had sent a summary of the day’s market activity. The fund’s portfolio had gained 0.4%, driven by a rally in emerging-market small caps. The trading algorithm had executed 932 transactions, none exceeding 0.005% of AUM. The SEC inquiry remained open but inactive. The NTA file was still marked “avoid.” The yakuza had not contacted her since Kinoshita’s note.

Everything was quiet. Too quiet.

She scrolled to the client database and searched for Nakamura Makoto. The file had an address: a small apartment in Shinjuku, near the Kabukichō district but far enough that it might be considered quiet. She zoomed in on the map. The building was a modest four-story walk-up, the kind where the landlord lived on the first floor and collected rent in cash.

She wondered what he did all day. If he woke up early or late. If he had friends or family. If he thought about the things he had done in the name of his country, or if he had managed to forget them.

She closed the app. The curiosity was dangerous. It made her want to find him, to see him, to remind herself that the fund was real and that the people she helped were real.

Instead, she called up a map of the park and stared at the trees.

Nakamura Makoto was seventy-eight years old, and he had been dead for a very long time.

Not legally dead—the Japanese government had no record of his death, because they had no record of his life. He did not exist in the population registry. He had never registered a My Number card. He had never filed a tax return. He had never visited a hospital under his own name. He was a ghost, walking through a city that did not know he was there.

He lived in a one-room apartment on the third floor of a building that had survived the war. The walls were thin, the floor was creaky, and the toilet was down the hall. But the rent was cheap—¥45,000 per month—and the landlord was a woman who had grown up with him in the same village in Fukushima, before he had disappeared into the world of intelligence and she had moved to Tokyo to marry a salaryman. She did not ask questions. She collected the cash and left him alone.

He spent his days walking.

He walked to the park in the morning, when the air was still cool and the only other people were old men like him, sitting on benches and staring at nothing. He walked to the convenience store at noon, buying a rice ball and a bottle of tea. He walked to the river in the evening, watching the water flow past, remembering the time he had crossed the border from East Berlin into West Berlin with a cassette tape hidden in his shoe.

He had been a courier once. That was the most romantic version of his former life. A man who carried secrets in his body, who traveled to dangerous places and brought back information that kept people safe. He had been told he was serving his country. He had been told he was fighting the Cold War. He had been told that his sacrifices would be remembered.

They had not. The Cold War ended. The Special Projects Division dissolved. His handlers retired or died. His file was sealed, then lost, then deliberately destroyed. The government forgot him. The world forgot him. He became a man without a past, living in a one-room apartment, collecting a pension from a fund that did not officially exist.

He did not know who ran the fund.

He had been contacted by a man—Chinese, clearly, though the accent was neutral—who had handed him a prepaid card and a letter explaining the terms. “You will receive ¥75,000 per month,” the letter said. “It will arrive on the first of every month. You do not need to do anything. You do not need to contact anyone. If you have questions, you will not receive answers.”

It had been six years. The payments had never missed a single month.

At first, Nakamura had been suspicious. He had worked in intelligence long enough to know that nothing was free. He expected the payments to stop. He expected someone to come asking for favors. He expected to be used again, the way he had been used by the Ministry, the way he had been used by the handlers who had taken him from the rural life and turned him into a weapon.

But no one came. The payments arrived. His landlord kept collecting the rent. He kept walking.

He stopped being suspicious. He started being grateful.

He did not know who ran the fund. He did not know if it was a person or an organization. He did not know if it was legal or illegal. He did not know if the money came from legitimate investments or from something darker.

He did not care.

He was sitting on his usual bench in the park when the young woman walked past.

She was in her mid-twenties, wearing a plain blouse and a skirt that looked like it came from a department store on sale. She carried a tote bag and a phone, and she walked with the hurried, distracted pace of someone who had a job to get back to. She did not notice him.

Nakamura watched her pass. He had been watching people for decades—it was a habit that had never broken. He noted the way she moved: confidently but not arrogantly. The way she held her phone: low, checking it occasionally, not glued to it. The way she glanced at the bench where he sat, then looked away, dismissing him as irrelevant.

He recognized the type. She was a normal person. She lived a normal life. She had no idea that the world was full of secrets, that the man on the bench had once carried a cassette tape in his shoe, that he had walked past armed guards in a foreign country and pretended to be a tourist.

And she had no idea that he received a pension from a fund that did not officially exist, that every month the money arrived like clockwork, that it made the difference between eating and starving.

He thought about the person who had set up the fund. He did not know if that person was a genius or a fool. He did not know if they were kind or cruel. He did not know if they would ever ask for something in return.

But he knew that they had given him a gift. A small one, perhaps—¥75,000 was not a fortune. But it was enough. It was enough to keep him in that one-room apartment, to buy his rice balls and tea, to keep the landlord quiet and the _koban_ police from asking questions.

It was enough to live.

He looked up at the sky, which was the pale blue of mid-June, and thought, _I hope they are happy._

He did not know who “they” were. He did not know if they were a single person or a committee, a human or a machine, a saint or a sinner.

But he hoped they were happy. Because the world needed more people who could make a ghost feel like he still existed.

Boushi returned to her apartment that night with a bowl of ramen from the convenience store and a stack of paperwork she had brought home from the insurance company.

She sat at her kitchen table, eating the ramen straight from the container, and opened her laptop. Komachi had flagged a potential issue: the medical sub-portfolio had been drawn down more than expected this month, with five emergency approvals in the past week. The AI recommended increasing the allocation by 0.1% of AUM to maintain the buffer.

Boushi approved the increase without comment. Then she opened the client database and looked at Nakamura Makoto’s file again.

She had never seen him. She would never see him. That was the point.

But she imagined him sitting in a park, looking up at the sky, feeling the warmth of the sun on his face. She imagined him opening the envelope with the prepaid card, counting the money, putting it away for another month. She imagined him living his life, quietly, peacefully, because someone had decided that he deserved to live.

She closed the laptop and ate her ramen.

The servers hummed. The traffic sounded outside. The world kept turning.

Somewhere in Shinjuku, an old man who did not exist was having dinner. He was eating a rice ball and drinking green tea. He was reading a newspaper that was two days old. He was listening to the radio, a classical station that played songs from before the war.

He was at peace.

And so, for just a moment, was she.

## Continuity Notes

- **Client Nakamura Makoto introduced:** 78-year-old former Cold War informant, ex-courier for MITI's dissolved Special Projects Division. He has no legal identity. Receives ¥75,000/month from the fund. He has never contacted the fund and does not know who runs it. Enrolled six years ago via the Chinese spy who provided the seed capital. His current state: peaceful acceptance.
- **Boushi's day job:** She processes insurance claims at a company that denies legitimate payouts. She deliberately approves a denied claim for a deceased policyholder, acting as a vigilante within the system.
- **No direct contact rule reinforced:** Boushi does not meet Nakamura; she only monitors via file. The chapter cuts to his POV briefly to show the impact of the fund's work.
- **Subplot updates:**
- **Yakuza:** No direct action this chapter, but Komachi notes no recent contact. Status quo maintained per Chapter 6.
- **SEC inquiry:** Still open but inactive. Boushi checks it casually; no new developments.
- **NTA audit:** File still marked "avoid." Komachi mentions no change. Auditor Sato Kenji's behavior not flagged as immediate threat.
- **Medical sub-portfolio:** Boushi approves a 0.1% AUM increase to maintain the buffer after several emergency payouts.
- **Thematic resonance:** Nakamura's peace mirrors Boushi's own rare moment of calm. The chapter ends on a quiet, hopeful note.
- **Character state:** Boushi remains detached but momentarily satisfied. Nakamura is stable. No new threats introduced.
