Chapter 26: Chapter 26: Epilogue: 山田彼人

# Chapter 26: Epilogue: 山田彼人

The steam rose from the pot in lazy curls.

Boushi stirred the miso soup with a wooden ladle, watching the tofu cubes tumble through the pale amber liquid. The kitchen was small—the same kitchen she had used for eight years, with the same faded curtains and the same chipped sink—but it smelled of home. Of simmering stock and fresh vegetables. Of something made with intention.

She added a pinch of dashi powder, tasted, added another.

The rice cooker clicked, signalling the end of its cycle. The fresh, clean scent of steamed rice filled the air.

On the cutting board, a fillet of salmon awaited its turn in the pan. She had bought it this morning at the fish market, not on discount. Full price. ¥780 for a single fillet.

She had not even hesitated.

*This is what normal feels like,* she thought again. But now, it felt true.

A door opened behind her.

Heavy footsteps. A deep, tired sigh.

"Something smells good."

Shota appeared in the kitchen doorway, still wearing his work shirt, the top two buttons undone. His hair was rumpled, his tie slung over his shoulder like a defeated flag.

"Accounting firm in Shinjuku" had become "senior partner at the same firm" over the years. The hours were still long, but the pay was better. Enough to afford salmon on a Tuesday.

"Go wash up," Boushi said, not turning from the stove. "Dinner will be ready in ten minutes."

He stepped closer, wrapped his arms around her waist, and pressed a kiss to the top of her head.

"How was your day?"

"Quiet. Komachi flagged two anomalous trades. I approved them. Someone in Venezuela bought a thousand shares of a Japanese robotics company through a shell."

"That happens?"

"It happens more than you'd think."

He laughed, released her, and headed for the bathroom.

Boushi smiled at the stove.

The apartment was different now.

Not the walls or the floor—those were the same—but the space itself. It had filled. With a second chair at the table. With a bookshelf in the corner, stacked with Shota's tax textbooks and Boushi's worn copies of Murakami. With a small kotatsu in the living room, where they sat together on cold winter nights.

And with the baby things.

The crib in the corner of the bedroom. The stack of diapers on the changing table. The mobile of paper cranes that Shota had folded himself, one by one, during the last month of the pregnancy.

The room that had once held only a futon and a laptop now held a family.

"Where is he?" Shota called from the bathroom.

"Sleeping. He went down about twenty minutes ago."

"Should we wake him?"

"No. We'll eat in peace. He'll wake up when he's hungry."

Shota emerged, his face damp, his hair slicked back. "I can't believe we have a baby."

"I can't believe we named him that."

Shota grinned. "It was your idea."

"It was your suggestion."

"Which you approved."

"Under duress."

"You laughed when I said it."

"I laughed because I thought you were joking."

They had this argument often. It always ended the same way—with Shota laughing, Boushi pretending to be annoyed, and both of them secretly pleased with the choice.

The name was registered at the ward office three weeks after the birth.

They had stood at the counter, a small piece of paper between them. The clerk—a middle-aged woman with reading glasses and the weary expression of someone who had seen every permutation of Japanese naming—had taken the form without looking at it.

"Name of the child?" she asked, her pen poised.

"Yamada Karehito," Shota said.

The clerk's pen stopped.

She looked down at the form. She looked up at Shota. She looked at Boushi.

She sighed.

"Yamada... *Karehito*."

"Yes."

"With the characters 彼人."

"Yes."

"彼 as in 'that' and 人 as in 'person.'"

"Yes."

The clerk exhaled through her nose. She adjusted her glasses.

"May I ask... the meaning behind this name?"

Shota glanced at Boushi. She gave a tiny nod.

"His mother has a... complicated history with names," Shota said. "She went her whole life being called a placeholder—a name that meant 'anonymous.' We wanted our son to have a name that acknowledged that. That said: *you are not a placeholder. You are a person. A specific person.*"

"He is *that* person," Boushi said quietly. "The one who matters."

The clerk stared at them.

"It's unusual," she said.

"We know."

"It will invite questions. Comments. Possibly teasing."

"We know."

She looked at the form again. She sighed again.

Then she stamped it.

"Congratulations. The registration is complete."

In the waiting area of the ward office, an elderly man had leaned over to his wife and whispered, loud enough for everyone to hear: *"彼人? What kind of name is that? Sounds like a character in a bad novel."*

Boushi had heard him.

She had smiled.

Now, in the kitchen, she ladled the miso soup into two bowls.

"The fund sent a dividend today," she said.

"Oh? How much?"

"Seven hundred and thirty-two million yen."

Shota paused, a chopstick halfway to his mouth. "Seven hundred and thirty-two million yen."

"Distributed across all accounts. The fee split is automatic. My share was about three hundred thousand."

"That's... still a lot."

"It's enough."

She carried the bowls to the table. Shota followed with the rice and the salmon.

They sat across from each other, the way they had first sat in the konbini eight years ago. The same quiet comfort. The same understanding that no words were necessary.

Boushi picked up her chopsticks.

"Thank you for dinner," Shota said.

"Thank you for eating it."

They began to eat.

Later, after the dishes were washed and the baby had been fed and changed and rocked back to sleep, Boushi stood in the doorway of the bedroom.

The plaque hung on the wall above the crib.

It was the same plaque that had hung in her old apartment. The one Kinoshita had asked about, years ago. The one that read:

**年金は人権なり**
*Pensions are human rights.*

She had considered taking it down when they moved here. It was old, faded, the paint chipped at the edges. Not the kind of thing you hung in a nursery.

But she had kept it.

Because the plaque was not just a slogan. It was a promise. And she wanted her son to grow up under that promise.

*You will be cared for. You will be protected. You will not be forgotten.*

She touched the frame gently.

In the living room, Shota was reading a book on the sofa. He looked up when she entered.

"Is he asleep?"

"Out cold. He'll be up again at three."

"I'll take the next feeding."

"You always say that."

"I mean it this time."

"You said that last time too, and then you slept through his crying."

"I was exhausted."

"You were pretending to be exhausted."

"I was genuinely exhausted."

She sat down next to him, letting her weight fall against his shoulder.

"Thank you," she said.

"For what?"

"For existing. For marrying me. For naming our son something ridiculous."

"I didn't marry you for your normal name."

"No. You married me for my pension fund."

"Also the karaage."

She laughed. It was a real laugh—full, warm, unguarded.

She had not realized, until this moment, how rare that sound had been in her life.

The clock on the wall read 11:14 PM.

Boushi's eyes were heavy. She had not worked today—not on the fund, not on the claims. She had spent the day at home, cooking, cleaning, holding her son.

It was the most productive day she had had in months.

She felt her phone buzz in her pocket.

A message from Komachi:

`All systems nominal. 19,931 stocks held. MER collected. No security anomalies. Medical sub-portfolio balance: ¥12.3 billion. Night cycle active.`

She typed back:

`Good night, Komachi.`

`Good night, Manager. Sleep well.`

She put the phone on the coffee table.

The fund ran itself. It had run itself for years now. The AI was stable. The security was tight. The yakuza kept their end of the bargain, and the Diet kept its distance, and the world had moved on to other crises.

Boushi had become a footnote in financial history—the woman who ran the world's most unusual pension fund from a konbini.

But here, in this small apartment, with her husband's arm around her and her son sleeping under a faded plaque, she was not a footnote.

She was home.

The next morning, she woke early.

The sun was just beginning to lighten the sky, casting a pale gray glow through the curtains. The apartment was quiet.

She padded into the kitchen, filled the kettle, set it to boil.

On the counter, a single onigiri sat in a plastic wrapper—tuna mayo, marked down from ¥120 to ¥80.

She picked it up, looked at it.

Then she put it in the trash.

She opened the refrigerator and took out a carton of eggs, a bunch of scallions, a block of tofu.

Today, she would make breakfast from scratch.

The kettle began to boil.

From the bedroom, she heard a small sound—a gurgle, then a cry.

The baby was awake.

She smiled.

She turned off the kettle, wiped her hands on her apron, and walked toward the bedroom.

Through the doorway, the plaque caught the morning light.

**年金は人権なり.**

*Pensions are human rights.*

And somewhere, across the city, in an office on the thirty-second floor of a building in Marunouchi, a banker named Michael Chen looked at his screen and saw a familiar name appear on a trade report.

He smiled.

He closed the file.

He did not investigate.

Some things, he had learned, were better left alone.

Boushi lifted her son from the crib.

He blinked up at her, his eyes still unfocused, his tiny fists waving in the air.

"Good morning, 山田彼人," she said.

He cooed.

She held him close, felt the warmth of his body against her chest.

The fund was running. The plaque was hanging. The miso soup was waiting.

And Yamada Boushi, whose name meant *Anonymous*, was home.

## Continuity Notes

- **Time skip:** Approximately 8 years after the trial. Boushi is now in her mid-30s. She is married to Tanaka Shota (the salaryman from Chapter 25). They have a baby son named 山田彼人 (Yamada Karehito). The name is a deliberate inversion of Boushi's own name—彼人 means "that person," a specific person, rather than the placeholder "某氏" (Anonymous). The naming joke is that it sounds odd to the registrar, but Boushi and Shota love it.

- **Emotional resolution:** Boushi has found domestic peace. She cooks fresh food (no more spoiled onigiri), has a family, and maintains the fund as an autopilot operation. The plaque "年金は人権なり" still hangs in the nursery. The chapter ends with her holding her son, content.

- **Fund status:** The fund continues to operate automatically. Komachi manages 19,931 stocks. The medical sub-portfolio is at ¥12.3 billion. No security anomalies. The fund is stable and self-sustaining. Boushi rarely needs to intervene.

- **Subplot conclusions:**
- **Yakuza:** Implicitly stable. The fund's continued smooth operation confirms the Ryujin-kai alliance remains intact. No further contact shown.
- **NTA audit:** Not referenced, but the lack of interference suggests the "avoid" order persisted. Auditor Sato Kenji never acted.
- **BlackRock/whisper:** Michael Chen sees a trade report with the fund's name and chooses not to investigate. He has accepted the fund's existence as something "better left alone." The external threat has dissipated.
- **Diet passive stance:** Not directly shown, but the fund's unbothered operation confirms Diet protection is still in place.
- **Romance:** Resolved. Boushi and Shota married and have a child.

- **Character state changes:**
- Boushi: Fully transitioned to family life. She no longer eats discounted onigiri; she cooks fresh food. She smiles freely.
- Shota: Now a senior partner at his accounting firm. Still kind, still warm.
- Komachi: Still operational.
- The fund: Now completely self-running. Boushi only checks in occasionally.

- **Thematic closure:** The epilogue reinforces the novel's core message—that pensions are human rights, that one person's determination can build something lasting, and that anonymous people can find a home. Boushi, who was once a placeholder, is now surrounded by specific, loved people.
